Term in Debt Acknowledgment under Kafalah: Whose Word Prevails?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on term acknowledgment in kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) accepts the debtor's word regarding a term in a debt acknowledgment, and contrast this with Imam Abu Yusuf's position.

Core Idea
In debt acknowledgment, the term is incidental; the acknowledger's word on it binds, not the creditor's denial.

This lesson examines a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school about the effect of a term (maturity date) when a debt is acknowledged. The core question is: when the debtor acknowledges a debt but claims it is due later, while the creditor insists it is immediate, whose word is accepted? The answer has significant practical consequences for when the debt becomes payable. We will explore the reasoning of Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad on one side, and Imam Abu Yusuf on the other, and understand the underlying principle that term is incidental to the original acknowledgment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Issue
The dispute is about whose statement regarding the term (mi'ad) is accepted: the debtor (muqirr) or the creditor (muqarr lahu). Both parties agree on the debt amount but disagree on whether it is payable immediately or after a term.
2
State the Majority Hanafi Position
Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad hold that the debtor's word is accepted. Their reasoning: when the debtor acknowledges a debt with a term, the term is part of his acknowledgment; it is an incidental condition. Since his acknowledgment is binding on himself, his statement about the incidental term is also binding, provided he is not contradicted by clear evidence.
The position is considered mufta bihi.
3
Present Abu Yusuf's Objection and His Qiyas
Imam Abu Yusuf analogizes to a different scenario in kafalah: if the kafil and the creditor agree on the debt amount but disagree on the term, the creditor's word is accepted because the kafil cannot impose a term without evidence. Here the creditor is the owner of the right, so his denial of the term prevails. Abu Yusuf extends this reasoning to the debtor's direct acknowledgment.
Abu Yusuf's qiyas: both cases involve a disagreement on term; the owner of the right (creditor) should have his word accepted.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response Rejecting the Qiyas
The Hanafi response (as in the text) argues that the analogy is invalid because the two cases are fundamentally different. In the debtor's acknowledgment, the term is incidental to his own admission; he is not adding a term to a pre-existing debt but acknowledging a debt that he himself defines with a term. In the kafalah case, the kafil is confirming a debt already established between the principal debtor and creditor, and the term is a separate claim. Therefore, the qiyas is fasid (invalid).
The text also criticizes Imam Shafi'i's qiyas in the opposite direction as equally invalid for similar reasons.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence and Mufta Bihi
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) is that under the majority Hanafi view, if the debtor claims a term and the creditor denies it, the debtor's word is taken (with an oath if the creditor demands it), so the debt is not immediately due. Under Abu Yusuf's view, the creditor's word would prevail, making the debt immediately due. The mufta bihi is the majority position.
This ruling applies both in debt acknowledgment and in kafalah when the kafil acknowledges a debt with a term.

Source Text

پس جب میعادی قرضہ کا اقرار کیا تو دوسری قسم یعنی غیر میعادی قرضہ لازم نہ ہوگا۔
اشرف البدایہ جلد نمیم
بلکہ میعادی قرضہ لازم ہوگا اور جب مقر پر میعادی قرضہ لازم ہوا تو مقر کا قول ہی معتبر ہو گا جیسے کفالہ کی صورت میں مقر یعنی کفیل کا قول معتبر ہوتا ہے حضرت امام ابو یوسف کے قول کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ دوسرے مسئلہ میں کفیل اور مکفول لۂ دونوں نے مال واجب ہونے پر اتفاق کیا ہے یعنی کفیل بھی اقرار کرتا ہے کہ مکفول عنہ پر ایک سو درہم ہیں اور میں کفیل ہوں اور مکفول لہ بھی اس کی تصدیق کرتا ہے پھر ان میں سے ایک یعنی کفیل نے میعاد کا دعوی کیا اور مکفول لڑ نے اس کا انکار کیا اور مدعی اینی کفیل کے پاس چونکہ بینہ موجود نہیں ہے اس لئے منکر یعنی مکفول لہ کا قول معتبر ہو گا جیسا کہ قرضہ کے اقرار کی صورت میں مقرلہ کا قول معتبر ہوتا ہے۔ ہماری طرف سے جواب یہ ہے کہ پہلے مسئلہ کو دوسرے مسئلہ پر قیاس کرنا جیسا کہ امام شافعی نے کیا ہے اور دوسرے مسئلہ کو پہلے مسئلہ پر قیاس کرنا جیسا کہ امام ابو یوسف نے کیا ہے دونوں فاسد ہیں۔ کیونکہ قرضہ کے اندر میعاد ایک عارضی چیز ہے اور اس کا دعوی اصل کے موافق ہے پس اس فرق کے ساتھ ایک کو دوسرے پر قیاس کرنا کس طرح درست ہو گا۔
Book of Kafalah. 1. With term. 2. Without term. 208. So when a debt with a term is acknowledged, the other type, i.e., debt without term, will not become binding. Rather, the debt with a term becomes binding. And when a debt with a term becomes binding on the acknowledger, his word alone is accepted, just as in the case of kafalah the word of the acknowledger, i.e., the kafil, is accepted. The reason for the statement of Hadrat Imam Abu Yusuf is that in the other issue, both the kafil and the makful lahu agreed that the wealth is owed, i.e., the kafil also acknowledges that the makful 'anhu owes one hundred dirhams and I am the surety, and the makful lahu confirms this. Then one of them, the kafil, claimed a term, and the makful lahu denied it. Since the claimant (the kafil) does not have evidence, the denier's (the makful lahu's) word is accepted, just as in the case of debt acknowledgment the word of the muqarr lahu is accepted. Our response is that analogizing the first issue to the second, as Imam Shafi'i did, and analogizing the second issue to the first, as Imam Abu Yusuf did, are both invalid because term in a debt is an incidental matter, and its claim is in accordance with the original. So with this difference, how can one be analogized to the other?
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage deals with a dispute within the Hanafi school (and with Imam Shafi'i) regarding the effect of a term (maturity) when a debt is acknowledged in the context of suretyship (kafalah). The Hanafi position (Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad) holds that when someone acknowledges a debt with a specified term, only that term debt is binding, and the acknowledger's word about the term is accepted. Imam Abu Yusuf analogizes this to a different case where both the kafil and the creditor agree on the debt amount but disagree on the term; there, the creditor's word is accepted because the kafil cannot impose a term without evidence. The author (Ashraf al-Hidaya) rejects both analogies (Shafi'i's and Abu Yusuf's) as invalid because the term is incidental to the debt claim; the debtor's acknowledgment of the debt itself implies the term if he states it, so his word is accepted on that incidental detail.

Key Terms
مقر (muqirr): The person who acknowledges (the debtor, or in kafalah, the kafil who acknowledges the debt and suretyship).
مقر له (muqarr lahu): The person in whose favor the acknowledgment is made (the creditor).
میعادی (mi'adi): With a specified term (maturity date).
بینہ (bayyinah): Clear evidence or proof, typically two witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi position (Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad), whose word is accepted regarding the term when a debtor acknowledges a debt with a term?
A) The debtor's word, because the term is part of his acknowledgment.
B) The creditor's word, because the debt is his right.
C) The debtor's word only if he produces witnesses.
D) Neither; the court decides based on custom.

Key Takeaway
When a debt is acknowledged with a term, the debtor's word on the term is accepted because the term is incidental to his own admission; this is the mufta bihi Hanafi position over Abu Yusuf's contrary analogy.

The Darak Surety: When Does the Buyer's Claim Arise?
Hanafi ruling on the sequence of decrees for darak surety liability, with intra-Hanafi difference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a buyer can claim the price from a kafil bil-darak, explain the proof from the continued validity of the sale, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
The buyer cannot claim from the darak surety until the judge orders the seller to return the price – mere ownership adjudication does not rescind the sale.

The darak guarantee is a form of suretyship where a third party promises to return the purchase price if the item is evicted by a rightful owner. This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah presents the Hanafi ruling on when this surety's obligation arises, setting a two-step judicial process. It also contrasts with the immediate voiding upon a freedom decree and records Imam Abu Yusuf's dissenting view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A buyer purchases a slave girl; a third party becomes a kafil bil-darak. A rightful claimant appears and the judge decrees the property to him. The question: can the buyer immediately claim the price from the kafil?
2
State the ruling from al-Quduri
According to the matn, the buyer cannot take from the kafil until the judge orders the seller to return the price. This is the zahir al-riwaya position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad).
The matn explicitly says 'lam ya'khudh al-kafil hatta yuqda lahu bithaman 'ala al-ba'i'.
3
Understand the proof (dalil)
The proof is that the sale is not rescinded by mere adjudication of ownership (istihqaq). The text provides a rational argument: the original owner may have permitted the sale, so the seller's liability for the price does not arise until a separate decree orders its return.
The example of the seller freeing a slave after the decree illustrates that the sale was still effective.
4
Contrast with the decree of freedom
If the slave girl is decreed free, the sale is voided immediately because the subject matter becomes non-existent. In that case, the buyer can claim from both seller and kafil immediately. This contrast sharpens the rule.
The text uses 'bikhilaf al-qada bil-hurriyah' to emphasize the difference.
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Abu Yusuf holds that the sale is voided by the mere decree of ownership, so the buyer can claim from the kafil immediately. The text notes this is from al-Ziyadat and is not the relied-upon position.
The mufta bihi position is the zahir al-riwaya, as stated in the commentary.

جواب اس کا جواب اور ان دونوں کے درمیان وجہ فرق یہ ہے کہ جب قاضی نے باندی کے آزاد ہونے کا حکم دیدیا تو باندی چونکہ محل بیج نہ رہی اس لئے باندی کی بیع باطل ہو جائے گی اور جب بیع باطل ہوگئی تو مشتری اپنا من بائع سے بھی واپس لے سکتا ہے اور کفیل سے بھی وصول کر سکتا ہے اور استحقاق کا حکم کرنے سے چونکہ بیع فسخ نہیں ہوتی۔ اس لئے محض استحقاق کا حکم کرنے کے بعد مشتری نہ بائع سے ثمن واپس لینے کا مجاز ہے اور نہ نفیل سے مطالبہ کرنے کا مجاز ہے۔
حضرت امام ابو یوسف سے امالی میں روایت ہے کہ قاضی کے محض استحقاق کا حکم کرنے سے بیع باطل ہو جاتی ہے اور جب محض استحقاق کا حکم کرنے سے بیع باطل ہو جاتی ہے تو اس روایت کے مطابق مشتری کو کفیل سے مطالبہ کا حق حاصل ہوگا اگر چہ قاضی نے بائع پر شمن کی واپسی کا حکم نہ کیا ہو۔ یہ مسئلہ امام محمد کی ترتیب دادہ زیادات کے اوائل میں ہے۔
فوائد حضر الام ابو یوسف اور اہم محمد جن مسائل کا املاء کراتے اور ابواب متعین فرماتے ان مسائل کے مجموعہ کا نام امالی ہے جو درحقیقت امام ابو یوسف کی تصنیف ہے اور کچھ مسائل جو ان ابواب سے متعلق تھے ان کا اضافہ امام محمد نے اپنی طرف سے کیا ہے ان مسائل کے
Someone bought a slave girl, then a person became a guarantor of the price (darak) for him. A rightful claimant appeared. Can the buyer take from the surety or not? He (al-Quduri) said: Whoever buys a slave girl and a man becomes a surety for him for darak, and she is claimed by a rightful owner, he (the buyer) cannot take from the surety until the price is decreed for him against the seller, because by mere claim of ownership the sale is not rescinded according to the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya) until the price is decreed for him against the seller. So when the return of the price is not obligatory on the principal (the seller), it is not obligatory on the surety. Unlike the decree of freedom, because the sale is voided by it due to the lack of subject matter, so he can recourse against the seller and the surety. And from Abu Yusuf it is narrated that the sale is voided by the claim of ownership; so according to his statement, he can recourse immediately upon the claim. Its place is in the early part of al-Ziyadat in the arrangement of the original. Translation: And if someone bought a slave girl, then a person became a guarantor of darak for him, then the slave girl became claimed, then the buyer cannot take from the surety until the decree of price against the seller is made for the buyer. Because according to zahir al-riwaya, the sale is not broken by mere claim of ownership until the decree of price for the buyer is made against the seller. So as long as the return of the price is not obligatory on the principal (the seller), it is not obligatory on the surety. Unlike the decree of freedom, because the sale becomes void by the decree of freedom, since the subject matter ceases to exist. So the buyer can recourse against both the seller and the surety. And it is narrated from Imam Abu Yusuf that the sale becomes void by the claim of ownership; so on the analogy of his statement, the buyer recourses immediately upon the claim. This issue is mentioned in the early part of al-Ziyadat in the original arrangement. Explanation: The scenario is that a person bought a slave girl, then another person became a surety of darak for the buyer, meaning he said to the buyer: If someone establishes a claim of ownership and takes this slave girl from you, I am the surety of your price. Then the slave girl became claimed, meaning someone... (continued on next page) ...claimed ownership and proved it, and the judge decreed the slave girl to the claimant. Then the buyer's right to demand the price from the surety is only when the judge orders the seller to return the price. And until the judge orders the seller to return the price, the buyer does not have the right to demand from the surety. And when the judge orders the seller to return the price, the buyer has the option to demand from either the seller or the surety. Then if the surety was a surety by the order of the seller, he will take back from the seller. The proof is that according to zahir al-riwaya, the sale is not rescinded by the judge's decree of ownership for the claimant; rather, the sale will be rescinded only when the judge orders the seller to return the price, because it is possible that the claimant gave permission to the seller to sell. For example, if the price of the slave girl was a slave, and the seller freed that slave after the judge's decree for the claimant, that slave would be freed from the seller's side. So when the sale is not rescinded by the decree of ownership for the claimant, then the return of the price is not obligatory on the principal (the seller), and thus it is not obligatory on the surety. And when it is not obligatory on the surety, the buyer does not have the right to demand from the surety. However, if the judge orders the seller to return the price, the sale is rescinded and the return of the price becomes obligatory on the seller, and therefore it becomes obligatory on the surety. In any case, it is established that after the decree of ownership for the claimant, until the judge orders the seller to return the price, the buyer is not entitled to demand the price from the surety. The phrase 'Unlike the decree of freedom' is a response to a question. The question is: In this same issue, if the slave girl claimed her freedom and proved it with witnesses, and the judge decreed her freedom, then the buyer does not have the right to demand the price from the surety. So what is the difference between the two? The answer is that when the judge decrees the freedom of the slave girl, the slave girl is no longer a valid subject matter of sale, so the sale becomes void. And when the sale becomes void, the buyer can take his price from the seller and also from the surety. But in the case of the claim of ownership, the sale is not rescinded by the decree, so after the mere decree of ownership, the buyer cannot take the price from the seller nor from the surety. It is narrated from Imam Abu Yusuf in al-Amali that the mere decree of ownership voids the sale. And according to that narration, the buyer has the right to demand from the surety even if the judge did not order the seller to return the price. This issue is in the early part of al-Ziyadat arranged by Imam Muhammad. Benefits: The collection of issues that Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad dictated and assigned chapters to is called al-Amali, which is actually the work of Imam Abu Yusuf, and Imam Muhammad added some issues related to those chapters.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school on the liability of a kafil bil-darak (guarantor against eviction). The case: a buyer purchases a slave girl, and a third party guarantees the price if the girl is claimed by a rightful owner. When the rightful owner appears and the judge decrees the girl to him, the buyer cannot immediately claim the price from the surety. According to the zahir al-riwaya (the well-known transmitted position of Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad), the sale is not rescinded by the mere decree of rightful ownership; rescission occurs only when the judge also orders the seller to return the price. Until then, the seller is not obligated to return the price, so the surety's obligation does not arise. In contrast, if the slave girl is decreed free (e.g., she proves she was free), the sale is voided immediately because the subject matter ceases to exist, so the buyer can claim from both seller and surety. Imam Abu Yusuf holds a different view: the sale is voided by the mere decree of rightful ownership, so the buyer can claim from the surety immediately. The text also notes that this issue is recorded in al-Ziyadat, and provides an anecdote about the compilation of al-Amali by Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Key Terms
درک (darak): Eviction guarantee; a surety against defects in title, ensuring the buyer gets the price back if the property is claimed by a rightful owner.
کفیل بالدرک (kafil bil-darak): The person who guarantees the price in case the purchased item is claimed by a rightful owner.
مستحق (mustahiqq): The person who establishes rightful ownership (by proof) and takes the property from the buyer.
ظاہر الروایہ (zahir al-riwayah): The well-known transmitted rulings from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani in the six books (al-Mabsut, al-Jami' al-Saghir, etc.).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (zahir al-riwaya), when can the buyer claim the price from the kafil bil-darak?
A) Immediately after the judge decrees the property to the rightful owner.
B) Only after the judge also orders the seller to return the price.
C) After the buyer sues the seller and loses.
D) Never; the kafil is not liable for darak.

Key Takeaway
The kafil bil-darak's obligation is contingent on a separate decree ordering the seller to return the price, because the sale survives the mere adjudication of ownership.

Kafalah — Surety

When Does the Surety Become Liable After Istihqaq?
Ruling with proof (istihsan) on surety liability after istihqaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a surety becomes liable after a qadi's decree of istihqaq and explain the reasoning (istihsan) behind it.

Core Idea
The qadi's decree of ownership does not automatically make the surety liable; only an order to return the price does.

In this lesson, we examine a scenario where a buyer purchases a slave girl with a surety for the warranty of title. A third party proves ownership, and the qadi decrees the slave girl to the claimant. When can the buyer claim the price from the surety? The Hanafi school distinguishes between the decree of ownership and the order to return the price, based on istihsan. We will walk through the ruling and its proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The buyer (mushtari) purchases a slave girl, and a third party (kafil) acts as surety for the 'uhda (warranty of title). Later, a claimant proves ownership, and the qadi decrees the slave girl to the claimant.
This is a typical case of istihqaq — a third party claims the sold item belongs to them.
2
State the ruling of Zahir al-Riwayah
According to the authoritative transmission (Zahir al-Riwayah), the sale is not rescinded by the mere decree of ownership. The buyer can only demand the price from the surety after the qadi orders the seller to return the price.
Contrast with the freedom case (discussed later in the passage).
3
Analyze the proof (istihsan)
The reasoning is that the original owner might have authorized the sale, so the sale remains valid until the qadi explicitly orders the return of the price. This is an istihsan (juristic preference) based on probability.
The alternative (qiyas) would be that the sale is void immediately, but istihsan prevails.
4
Determine the buyer's rights
After the qadi orders the return of the price, the buyer has the option to claim from either the seller or the surety. If the surety stood by the seller's order, the surety can recover from the seller.
The surety's liability is derivative of the seller's liability.

بخلاف القضاء بالحرية .... الخ سے ایک سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ اسی مسئلہ میں اگر باندی نے اپنے آزاد ہونے کا دعوی کرا کے گواہوں سے اس کو ثابت کر دیا اور قاضی نے اس کے آزاد ہونے کا حکم دیدیا تو مشتری کو کفیل سے مطالبہ ثمن کا حق حاصل نہیں ہوتا ۔ پس ان دونوں کے درمیان کیا فرق ہے؟
جواب اس کا جواب اور ان دونوں کے درمیان وجہ فرق یہ ہے کہ جب قاضی نے باندی کے آزاد ہونے کا حکم دیدیا تو باندی چونکہ محل بیع نہ رہی اس لئے باندی کی بیع باطل ہو جائے گی اور جب بیع باطل ہوگئی تو مشتری اپنا ثمن بائع سے بھی واپس لے سکتا ہے اور کفیل سے بھی وصول کر سکتا ہے اور استحقاق کا حکم کرنے سے چونکہ بیع فسخ نہیں ہوتی۔ اس لئے محض استحقاق کا حکم کرنے کے بعد مشتری نہ بائع سے ثمن واپس لینے کا مجاز ہے اور نہ کفیل سے مطالبہ کرنے کا مجاز ہے۔
حضرت امام ابو یوسف سے امالی میں روایت ہے کہ قاضی کے محض استحقاق کا حکم کرنے سے بیع باطل ہو جاتی ہے اور جب محض استحقاق کا حکم کرنے سے بیع باطل ہو جاتی ہے تو اس روایت کے مطابق مشتری کو کفیل سے مطالبہ کا حق حاصل ہوگا اگر چہ قاضی نے بائع پر ثمن کی واپسی کا حکم نہ کیا ہو۔ یہ مسئلہ امام محمد کی ترتیب دادہ زیادات کے اوائل میں ہے۔
فوائد حضرت امام ابو یوسف اور امام محمد جن مسائل کا املاء کراتے اور ابواب متعین فرماتے ان مسائل کے مجموعہ کا نام امالی ہے جو درحقیقت امام ابو یوسف کی تصنیف ہے اور کچھ مسائل جو ان ابواب سے متعلق تھے ان کا اضافہ امام محمد نے اپنی طرف سے کیا ہے ان مسائل کے مجموعہ کا نام " زیادات " ہے جو در حقیقت امام محمد کی تصنیف ہے۔ امام ابو یوسف نے جب اس کتاب کا املاء کرایا تو اس کا آغاز کتاب الماذون سے فرمایا تھا امام محمد نے بھی اس ترتیب کو تبدیل نہیں فرمایا لیکن شیخ زعفرانی نے زیادات کی ترتیب بدل دی اور اس ترتیب پر مرتب فرمایا جس ترتیب پر زیادات فی زمانا موجود ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے اس مسئلہ کا حوالہ دیتے ہوئے فرمایا کہ یہ مسئلہ زیادات کے اوائل میں ہے لیکن زیادات سے مراد امام محمد کی ترتیب دادہ زیادات ہے شیخ زعفرانی کی ترتیب دادہ زیادات مراد نہیں ہے۔
When [someone] claims ownership and proves it, and the judge decrees the slave girl for the claimant, the buyer's right to demand the price from the surety arises only when the judge orders the seller to return the price. As long as the judge has not ordered the seller to return the price, the buyer has no right to demand from the surety. When the judge orders the seller to return the price, the buyer has the option to demand from the seller or the surety. Then if the surety stood surety by the seller's order, he will recover from the seller.
The proof is that according to Zahir al-Riwayah, the sale is not rescinded by the judge's decree of ownership for the claimant. Rather, the sale is rescinded when the judge orders the seller to return the price, because it is possible that the owner (claimant) permitted the seller to sell. Thus, if the price of the slave girl was a slave, and after the judge's decree the seller freed that slave for the owner, that slave becomes free on behalf of the seller. Since the sale is not rescinded by the decree of ownership, the principal (the seller) is not obligated to return the price, and when the principal is not obligated, the surety is not obligated to pay the price. Therefore, the buyer has no right to demand from the surety. However, if the judge orders the seller to return the price, the sale is rescinded and the seller is obligated to return the price, and then the surety becomes obligated. Thus it is established that after the decree of ownership, the buyer is not entitled to demand the price from the surety until the judge orders the seller to return the price.
As opposed to the decree of freedom... this answers a question. The question is: In the same issue, if the slave girl claimed her freedom and proved it with witnesses, and the judge decreed her freedom, then the buyer does not have the right to demand the price from the surety. What is the difference between the two?
Answer: The difference is that when the judge decreed the slave girl's freedom, she ceased to be a valid subject of sale, so the sale becomes void. When the sale is void, the buyer can recover his price from the seller and also from the surety. But in the case of ownership, the decree does not rescind the sale, so after merely a decree of ownership, the buyer is neither entitled to recover the price from the seller nor to demand from the surety.
There is a narration from Imam Abu Yusuf in al-Amali that the sale becomes void by the mere decree of ownership. According to that narration, the buyer would have the right to demand from the surety even if the judge did not order the return of the price. This issue is in the beginning of al-Ziyadat as arranged by Imam Muhammad.
Benefits: Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad used to dictate issues and specify chapters. The collection of those issues is called al-Amali, which is actually the work of Imam Abu Yusuf. Some additional issues related to those chapters were added by Imam Muhammad; that collection is called al-Ziyadat, which is actually the work of Imam Muhammad. When Imam Abu Yusuf dictated this book, he began with Kitab al-Ma'dhun. Imam Muhammad did not change this order, but Shaykh al-Za'farani changed the order of al-Ziyadat and arranged it in the order in which al-Ziyadat exists today. The author of al-Hidayah, when referring to this issue, said it is in the beginning of al-Ziyadat, meaning al-Ziyadat as arranged by Imam Muhammad, not by Shaykh al-Za'farani.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the chapter of Kafalah (suretyship) with its proof and an intra-Hanafi dispute. The ruling concerns a scenario where someone buys a slave girl, and a third party acts as surety for the 'uhda (warranty of title). Later, a claimant proves ownership of the slave girl before a qadi, who decrees the slave girl to the claimant. The question is: when can the buyer demand the price from the surety?

The passage clarifies that according to Zahir al-Riwayah (the authoritative transmission of Imam Muhammad), the mere decree of ownership does not rescind the sale. Therefore, the seller (asil) is not obligated to return the price until the qadi explicitly orders him to do so. Consequently, the surety is not liable either. The reasoning (dalil) is that the original owner might have authorized the sale, so the sale remains valid until the qadi orders the return of the price. Only after that order does the sale become rescinded and the surety liable.

The passage then contrasts this with the case of a slave girl who claims freedom and the qadi decrees her freedom. In that case, the sale becomes void immediately because a free person cannot be a valid subject of sale. Thus, the buyer can immediately reclaim the price from the seller or the surety.

Finally, an intra-Hanafi dispute is noted: Imam Abu Yusuf, in his book al-Amali, narrates that the sale becomes void by the mere decree of ownership, giving the buyer the right to demand from the surety immediately. This shows that even within the Hanafi school, there are variant narrations, but the relied-upon position (as per al-Hidayah) is the one from Zahir al-Riwayah. The passage also provides historical context about the sources al-Amali and al-Ziyadat and their arrangements.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istihqaq): Establishment of ownership claim by a third party over a sold item.
بیع (bay'): A contract of sale that may be rescinded (faskh) under certain conditions.
کفیل (kafil): Surety who guarantees the price in case of a title defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Immediately after the qadi decrees the slave girl to the claimant.
B) When the qadi orders the seller to return the price.
C) Only after the surety admits liability.
D) When the buyer files a separate lawsuit against the surety.

Key Takeaway
The surety's liability depends on the qadi's order to return the price, not on the decree of ownership alone.

Kafalah — Surety

Contrasting Istihqaq with Freedom Decree and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Comparison of two decrees and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the effects of an istihqaq (ownership) decree and a freedom decree on the sale, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the timing of rescission.

Core Idea
A freedom decree voids the sale immediately; an istihqaq decree does not. Imam Abu Yusuf disagrees, but the relied-upon position is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

In this lesson, we build on the previous ruling by comparing two scenarios: one where a qadi decrees a slave girl to a claimant (istihqaq), and another where the slave girl herself proves her freedom. The outcomes differ significantly. We also explore an intra-Hanafi difference: Imam Abu Yusuf's narration that the sale becomes void by the mere decree of ownership, contrasting with the Zahir al-Riwayah position. This lesson sharpens your ability to distinguish between related rulings.

Guided Path
1
Review the two cases
Case 1: Istihqaq decree – claimant proves ownership. Case 2: Freedom decree – slave girl proves freedom. Both are decrees that affect the buyer's right to claim from the surety.
The key difference is the nature of the decree: ownership vs. status change.
2
Compare the outcomes for the buyer
In istihqaq, the buyer cannot claim from surety until the qadi orders return of price. In freedom, the buyer can claim immediately without further order.
The reason: freedom destroys the subject of sale, making the sale void; ownership does not necessarily do so.
3
Analyze the reasoning behind the difference
In istihqaq, the original owner might have authorized the sale, so sale remains valid. In freedom, a free person cannot be sold, so sale is void ab initio.
This is based on the possibility of authorization in istihqaq, which does not exist in freedom.
4
Study the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Abu Yusuf (in al-Amali) holds that the istihqaq decree alone voids the sale, making the surety liable immediately. This opposes the Zahir al-Riwayah position.
The difference stems from whether the possibility of authorization is considered sufficient to keep the sale intact.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position (per al-Hidayah) is that of Zahir al-Riwayah: the sale is not voided by the mere decree, only by the order to return the price. Imam Abu Yusuf's view is a weaker narration.
The practical consequence: in fatwa, the surety is not liable until the order.

Source Text

استحقاق کا دعوی کر کے اس کو ثابت کر دیا اور قاضی نے مدعی استحقاق کے لئے باندی کا فیصلہ کر دیا تو مشتری کو کفیل سے ثمن کے مطالبہ کا حق اس وقت حاصل ہو گا جب قاضی مشتری کے لئے بائع پر ثمن واپس کرنے کا حکم کر دے اور جب تک قاضی مشتری کے واسطے بائع پر ثمن واپس کرنے کا حکم نہیں کرے گا۔ اس وقت تک مشتری کو کفیل سے مطالبہ کا حق حاصل نہ ہو گا اور جب قاضی نے مشتری کے واسطے بائع پر ثمن واپس کرنے کا حکم کیا تو مشتری کو اختیار ہے چاہے بائع سے مطالبہ کرے چاہے کفیل سے مطالبہ کرے۔ پھر اگر کفیل ، بائع کے حکم سے کفیل ہوا تھا تو وہ بائع سے واپس لے گا۔
دلیل یہ ہے کہ قاضی کے مستحق کے واسطے استحقاق کا حکم کرنے سے ظاہر الروایہ کے مطابق بیع نہیں ٹوٹتی ہے بلکہ بیع اس وقت ٹوٹے گی جب قاضی مشتری کے واسطے بائع پر ثمن واپس کرنے کا حکم کر دے گا کیونکہ ہوسکتا ہے کہ مستحق بائع کو بیچ کی اجازت دیدے۔ چنانچہ اگر باندی کا ثمن غلام ہوا اور بائع نے مستحق کے واسطے قاضی کے حکم کے بعد اس غلام کو آزاد کر دیا ہو تو وہ غلام بائع کی طرف سے آزاد ہو جائے گا پس جب مستحق کے واسطے استحقاق کا حکم کرنے سے بیع فسخ نہیں ہوتی تو اصیل یعنی بائع پر ثمن واپس کرنا بھی واجب نہیں ہوا اور جب اصیل یعنی بائع پر ثمن واپس کرنا واجب نہیں ہوا تو کفیل پر بھی مطالبہ ثمن واجب نہ ہوگا۔ اور جب کفیل پر مطالبہ ثمن واجب نہیں ہوا تو مشتری کو کفیل سے مطالبہ کا حق بھی حاصل نہ ہوگا۔ ہاں اگر قاضی ، بائع پر ثمن واپس کرنے کا حکم کر دے تو بیع فسخ ہو جائے گی اور بائع پر ثمن واپس کرنا واجب ہوگا اور جب بائع پر ثمن واپس کرنا واجب ہو گیا تو کفیل پر بھی مطالبہ ثمن واجب ہو جائے گا بہر حال یہ بات ثابت ہوگئی کہ مستحق کے واسطے استحقاق کا حکم کرنے کے بعد جب تک قاضی بائع پر ثمن واپس کرنے کا حکم نہیں کرے گا اس وقت تک مشتری کفیل سے مطالبہ ثمن کا مجاز نہ ہوگا۔
بخلاف القضاء بالحرية .... الخ سے ایک سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ اسی مسئلہ میں اگر باندی نے اپنے آزاد ہونے کا دعوی کرا کے گواہوں سے اس کو ثابت کر دیا اور قاضی نے اس کے آزاد ہونے کا حکم دیدیا تو مشتری کو کفیل سے مطالبہ ثمن کا حق حاصل نہیں ہوتا ۔ پس ان دونوں کے درمیان کیا فرق ہے؟
جواب اس کا جواب اور ان دونوں کے درمیان وجہ فرق یہ ہے کہ جب قاضی نے باندی کے آزاد ہونے کا حکم دیدیا تو باندی چونکہ محل بیع نہ رہی اس لئے باندی کی بیع باطل ہو جائے گی اور جب بیع باطل ہوگئی تو مشتری اپنا ثمن بائع سے بھی واپس لے سکتا ہے اور کفیل سے بھی وصول کر سکتا ہے اور استحقاق کا حکم کرنے سے چونکہ بیع فسخ نہیں ہوتی۔ اس لئے محض استحقاق کا حکم کرنے کے بعد مشتری نہ بائع سے ثمن واپس لینے کا مجاز ہے اور نہ کفیل سے مطالبہ کرنے کا مجاز ہے۔
حضرت امام ابو یوسف سے امالی میں روایت ہے کہ قاضی کے محض استحقاق کا حکم کرنے سے بیع باطل ہو جاتی ہے اور جب محض استحقاق کا حکم کرنے سے بیع باطل ہو جاتی ہے تو اس روایت کے مطابق مشتری کو کفیل سے مطالبہ کا حق حاصل ہوگا اگر چہ قاضی نے بائع پر ثمن کی واپسی کا حکم نہ کیا ہو۔ یہ مسئلہ امام محمد کی ترتیب دادہ زیادات کے اوائل میں ہے۔
فوائد حضرت امام ابو یوسف اور امام محمد جن مسائل کا املاء کراتے اور ابواب متعین فرماتے ان مسائل کے مجموعہ کا نام امالی ہے جو درحقیقت امام ابو یوسف کی تصنیف ہے اور کچھ مسائل جو ان ابواب سے متعلق تھے ان کا اضافہ امام محمد نے اپنی طرف سے کیا ہے ان مسائل کے مجموعہ کا نام " زیادات " ہے جو در حقیقت امام محمد کی تصنیف ہے۔ امام ابو یوسف نے جب اس کتاب کا املاء کرایا تو اس کا آغاز کتاب الماذون سے فرمایا تھا امام محمد نے بھی اس ترتیب کو تبدیل نہیں فرمایا لیکن شیخ زعفرانی نے زیادات کی ترتیب بدل دی اور اس ترتیب پر مرتب فرمایا جس ترتیب پر زیادات فی زمانا موجود ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے اس مسئلہ کا حوالہ دیتے ہوئے فرمایا کہ یہ مسئلہ زیادات کے اوائل میں ہے لیکن زیادات سے مراد امام محمد کی ترتیب دادہ زیادات ہے شیخ زعفرانی کی ترتیب دادہ زیادات مراد نہیں ہے۔
When [someone] claims ownership and proves it, and the judge decrees the slave girl for the claimant, the buyer's right to demand the price from the surety arises only when the judge orders the seller to return the price. As long as the judge has not ordered the seller to return the price, the buyer has no right to demand from the surety. When the judge orders the seller to return the price, the buyer has the option to demand from the seller or the surety. Then if the surety stood surety by the seller's order, he will recover from the seller.
The proof is that according to Zahir al-Riwayah, the sale is not rescinded by the judge's decree of ownership for the claimant. Rather, the sale is rescinded when the judge orders the seller to return the price, because it is possible that the owner (claimant) permitted the seller to sell. Thus, if the price of the slave girl was a slave, and after the judge's decree the seller freed that slave for the owner, that slave becomes free on behalf of the seller. Since the sale is not rescinded by the decree of ownership, the principal (the seller) is not obligated to return the price, and when the principal is not obligated, the surety is not obligated to pay the price. Therefore, the buyer has no right to demand from the surety. However, if the judge orders the seller to return the price, the sale is rescinded and the seller is obligated to return the price, and then the surety becomes obligated. Thus it is established that after the decree of ownership, the buyer is not entitled to demand the price from the surety until the judge orders the seller to return the price.
As opposed to the decree of freedom... this answers a question. The question is: In the same issue, if the slave girl claimed her freedom and proved it with witnesses, and the judge decreed her freedom, then the buyer does not have the right to demand the price from the surety. What is the difference between the two?
Answer: The difference is that when the judge decreed the slave girl's freedom, she ceased to be a valid subject of sale, so the sale becomes void. When the sale is void, the buyer can recover his price from the seller and also from the surety. But in the case of ownership, the decree does not rescind the sale, so after merely a decree of ownership, the buyer is neither entitled to recover the price from the seller nor to demand from the surety.
There is a narration from Imam Abu Yusuf in al-Amali that the sale becomes void by the mere decree of ownership. According to that narration, the buyer would have the right to demand from the surety even if the judge did not order the return of the price. This issue is in the beginning of al-Ziyadat as arranged by Imam Muhammad.
Benefits: Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad used to dictate issues and specify chapters. The collection of those issues is called al-Amali, which is actually the work of Imam Abu Yusuf. Some additional issues related to those chapters were added by Imam Muhammad; that collection is called al-Ziyadat, which is actually the work of Imam Muhammad. When Imam Abu Yusuf dictated this book, he began with Kitab al-Ma'dhun. Imam Muhammad did not change this order, but Shaykh al-Za'farani changed the order of al-Ziyadat and arranged it in the order in which al-Ziyadat exists today. The author of al-Hidayah, when referring to this issue, said it is in the beginning of al-Ziyadat, meaning al-Ziyadat as arranged by Imam Muhammad, not by Shaykh al-Za'farani.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the chapter of Kafalah (suretyship) with its proof and an intra-Hanafi dispute. The ruling concerns a scenario where someone buys a slave girl, and a third party acts as surety for the 'uhda (warranty of title). Later, a claimant proves ownership of the slave girl before a qadi, who decrees the slave girl to the claimant. The question is: when can the buyer demand the price from the surety?

The passage clarifies that according to Zahir al-Riwayah (the authoritative transmission of Imam Muhammad), the mere decree of ownership does not rescind the sale. Therefore, the seller (asil) is not obligated to return the price until the qadi explicitly orders him to do so. Consequently, the surety is not liable either. The reasoning (dalil) is that the original owner might have authorized the sale, so the sale remains valid until the qadi orders the return of the price. Only after that order does the sale become rescinded and the surety liable.

The passage then contrasts this with the case of a slave girl who claims freedom and the qadi decrees her freedom. In that case, the sale becomes void immediately because a free person cannot be a valid subject of sale. Thus, the buyer can immediately reclaim the price from the seller or the surety.

Finally, an intra-Hanafi dispute is noted: Imam Abu Yusuf, in his book al-Amali, narrates that the sale becomes void by the mere decree of ownership, giving the buyer the right to demand from the surety immediately. This shows that even within the Hanafi school, there are variant narrations, but the relied-upon position (as per al-Hidayah) is the one from Zahir al-Riwayah. The passage also provides historical context about the sources al-Amali and al-Ziyadat and their arrangements.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istihqaq): Establishment of ownership claim by a third party over a sold item.
بیع (bay'): A contract of sale that may be rescinded (faskh) under certain conditions.
کفیل (kafil): Surety who guarantees the price in case of a title defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad (Zahir al-Riwayah) and Imam Abu Yusuf regarding the effect of an istihqaq decree?
A) Under both views, the buyer cannot claim from the surety until the qadi orders return of price.
B) Under Imam Muhammad's view, the buyer can claim immediately; under Imam Abu Yusuf, he must wait.
C) Under Imam Muhammad's view, the buyer must wait for the order; under Imam Abu Yusuf, he can claim immediately.
D) Neither view allows the buyer to claim from the surety.

Key Takeaway
An istihqaq decree does not void the sale; a freedom decree does. Intra-Hanafi disagreement exists on the point, but the relied-upon position requires a further order for surety liability.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
Invalidity of Dhaman 'Uhda Due to Ambiguity
Ruling that dhaman 'uhda is invalid because the term 'uhda is ambiguous

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that dhaman 'uhda is invalid due to ambiguity of the term 'uhda, and distinguish it from dhaman darak which is valid by custom.

Core Idea
A guarantee with an ambiguous term is void – clarity of subject is essential for dhaman.

This passage establishes a key principle in Hanafi fiqh: a guarantee (dhaman) is valid only if the subject matter is clearly defined. The term 'uhda, often used in sales, is ambiguous – it can refer to the old deed, the contract, its rights, the indemnity (darak), or an option (khiyar). Because of this uncertainty, guaranteeing the 'uhda is invalid. In contrast, the term darak is well-established in custom for indemnity against ownership claims, so dhaman darak is valid by consensus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what legal act is being discussed (dhaman of 'uhda) and its ruling (invalid/batil).
The matn states: 'If someone buys a slave and then a man becomes surety for the 'uhda, this guarantee is invalid.'
2
Understand the reason (ta'lil)
Analyze why the ruling is invalid: because the term 'uhda is ambiguous (mushtabiha) and can refer to multiple things, causing jahalah (uncertainty) in the subject matter.
The text lists five possible meanings: old deed, contract, rights, darak, khiyar.
3
Contrast with dhaman darak
Note that dhaman darak is valid because the term darak has a specific customary meaning (guarantee against ownership claims), removing ambiguity.
The text says: '...unlike the term darak, because darak is customarily used for guarantee against ownership claims.'
4
Note the consensus
The commentary states that dhaman 'uhda is invalid by consensus (bil-ittifaq), and dhaman darak is valid by consensus.
This shows the ruling is agreed upon across the Hanafi school and likely other schools.

کسی نے ایک غلام خریدا پھر ایک آدمی عہدہ کا ضامن ہو گیا تو یہ ضمان باطل ہے
ومن اشترى عبدا فضمن له رجل بالعهدة فالضمان باطل لان هذه اللفظة مشتبهة قد تقع على الصك القديم وهو ملك البائع فلا يصح ضمانه وتقع على العقد على حقوقه وعلى الدرك وعلى الخيار ولكل ذلك وجه فتعذر العمل بها بخلاف الدرك لانه استعمل في ضمان الاستحقاق عرفا
ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے غلام خریدا پھر اس کے واسطے ایک آدمی عہدہ کا ضامن ہوگیا تو یہ ضمان باطل ہے کیونکہ یہ لفظ مشتبہ ہے کبھی عہدہ کا لفظ پرانی دستاویز پر بولا جاتا ہے حالانکہ یہ بائع کی ملک ہے پس اس کا ضمان صحیح نہیں ہے اور کبھی یہ لفظ عقد اور اس کے حقوق پر بولا جاتا ہے اور کبھی ضمان بالدرک پر اور کبھی خیار پر اور ہر ایک کے لئے وجہ موجود ہے پس اس پر عمل کرنا مستعد رہے برخلاف لفظ درک کے کیونکہ لفظ درک عرف میں ضمان استحقاق میں مستعمل
تشریح ضمان تین طرح کے ہیں،
۱ ضمان عہدہ
۲ ضمان درک
۳ ضمان خلاص
ضمان خلاص تو اگلی عبارت میں مذکور ہے اور ضمان عہدہ بالاتفاق باطل اور ضمان درک بالاتفاق جائز ہے۔ ضمان عہدہ کے باطل ہونے کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ لفظ عہدہ کی مراد مشتبہ اور مجہول ہے کیونکہ لفظ عہدہ بھی تو قدیم بیعنامہ اور پرانی دستاویز پر بولا جاتا ہے اور بھی عقد پر اور کبھی حقوق عقد پر اور کبھی درک پر اور کبھی خیار شرط پر بولا جاتا ہے پرانی دستاویز پر تو اس لئے بولا جاتا ہے کہ پرانی دستاویز کتاب العہد کے مانند ایک وثیقہ ہے پس اس وثیقہ کا نام عہدہ رکھ دیا گیا۔ اور عقد پر اس لئے بولا جاتا ہے کہ "عہدہ" عہد سے ماخوذ ہے اور عہد اور عقد چونکہ دونوں ایک ہیں اس لئے عہدہ کا لفظ عقد پر بھی بولا جاتا ہے اور حقوق عقد چونکہ عقد کے ثمرات میں سے ہیں اس لئے عہدہ کا لفظ حقوق عقد پر بھی بولا جاتا ہے اور خیار شرط اس لئے ہے کہ حدیث میں ہے عهدة الرفيق ثلاثة ايام یعنی غلام کا خیار شرط تین دن ہے لفظ عہدہ سے اگر قدیم بیعنامہ اور پرانی دستاویز مراد ہو تو مشتری کے واسطے بائع کی طرف سے اس دستاویز کا ضامن ہونا اس لئے باطل ہے کہ یہ دستاویز بائع کی ملک ہے اور بائع پر مضمون نہیں ہے چنانچہ اگر بائع کے پاس یہ دستاویز ہلاک ہوگئی تو بائع پر اس کا ضمان واجب نہیں ہوتا۔ اور پہلے گزر چکا کہ جو چیز اصیل (مکفول عنہ) پر مضمون نہ ہو اس کا کفالہ اور ضمان بھی صحیح نہیں ہوتا اس لئے اس دستاویز کا کفالہ درست نہ ہوگا۔
دوسری بات یہ ہے کہ یہ دستاویز جب بائع کی ملک ہے اور بائع کے قبضہ میں ہے تو کفیل اس کے سپرد کرنے پر قادر نہ ہوگا اور کفیل
If someone buys a slave and then a man becomes surety for him for the 'uhda, this guarantee is invalid. And whoever buys a slave and then a man becomes surety for him for the 'uhda, the guarantee is invalid because this term is ambiguous. It may refer to the old deed (al-sakk al-qadim), which is the property of the seller, so his guarantee is not valid. It may refer to the contract, its rights, to darak (indemnity), or to khiyar (option). For each there is a possibility, so acting upon it is difficult, unlike the term darak, because darak is customarily used for guarantee against ownership claims.
Translation: And if someone bought a slave and then a man became surety for him for the 'uhda, this guarantee is invalid because this term is ambiguous. Sometimes the word 'uhda is used for an old document, even though it is the seller's property, so its guarantee is not valid. Sometimes this word is used for the contract and its rights, and sometimes for guarantee by darak, and sometimes for khiyar, and for each there is a reason. So acting upon it is difficult, unlike the word darak, because the word darak is customarily used for guarantee of ownership claims.
Explanation: Guarantee (dhaman) is of three types:
1. Dhaman 'uhda
2. Dhaman darak
3. Dhaman khalas
Dhaman khalas will be mentioned in the following passage. Dhaman 'uhda is invalid by consensus, and dhaman darak is valid by consensus. The reason for the invalidity of dhaman 'uhda is that the meaning of the word 'uhda is ambiguous and unknown, because the word 'uhda is used for an old bill of sale and an old document, and also for the contract, and sometimes for the rights of the contract, and sometimes for darak, and sometimes for khiyar al-shart. It is used for the old document because the old document is a deed like the book of 'uhda, so that deed was named 'uhda. It is used for the contract because 'uhda is derived from 'ahd (covenant), and since 'ahd and 'aqd (contract) are the same, the word 'uhda is also used for the contract. Since the rights of the contract are among its fruits, 'uhda is also used for them. It is used for khiyar al-shart because there is a hadith: 'The 'uhda of a companion is three days,' meaning the khiyar al-shart of a slave is three days. If by the word 'uhda the old bill of sale or old document is meant, then the buyer's guarantee of that document from the seller is invalid because this document is the seller's property and is not guaranteed upon the seller. Thus, if that document is destroyed in the seller's possession, the seller is not liable for its guarantee. It has already been mentioned that something which is not guaranteed upon the principal (the one for whom the guarantee is made) cannot be the subject of a valid kafalah or guarantee. Therefore, the kafalah of this document is not valid. Secondly, since this document is the seller's property and in his possession, the surety is not able to hand it over.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement regarding the invalidity of dhaman 'uhda (warranty of 'uhda). The opening matn states that if a person buys a slave and another person becomes a surety for the 'uhda, this guarantee is invalid. The reason given is that the term 'uhda is ambiguous (mushtabiha) and can refer to multiple things: the old deed (which is the seller's property and not guaranteed), the contract itself, the rights of the contract, the indemnity (darak), or the option (khiyar). Because of this ambiguity, it is impossible to determine what exactly is being guaranteed, so the guarantee is void.

The passage then contrasts 'uhda with darak, noting that darak has a specific customary meaning (guarantee against ownership claims) and is therefore valid. The explanation (tashrih) categorizes guarantees into three types: dhaman 'uhda, dhaman darak, and dhaman khalas. Dhaman 'uhda is invalid by consensus (bil-ittifaq), while dhaman darak is valid by consensus. The reason for the invalidity is the ambiguity of the term. The explanation expands on the possible meanings of 'uhda, citing the hadith 'uhdat al-rafiq thalathat ayyam' to show that 'uhda can mean khiyar al-shart. Since the intended meaning in the guarantee contract is unclear, the contract is invalid.

Key Terms
عہدہ ('uhda): A term used for warranty of title, but ambiguous as it can refer to old deed, contract, rights, darak, or khiyar.
ضمان (dhaman): A guarantee or suretyship contract.
درک (darak): Customary term for guarantee against ownership claims (istihqaq).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is dhaman 'uhda considered invalid in the Hanafi school?

B) Because the term 'uhda is ambiguous and can refer to multiple things, causing uncertainty.
C) Because it is not mentioned in the Quran or Sunnah.
D) Because it is only valid for free persons, not slaves.

Key Takeaway
Dhaman 'uhda is invalid because the term 'uhda is ambiguous; dhaman darak is valid because darak has a clear customary meaning.

Ruling on Daman 'Uhdah and Daman Darak
States the invalidity of daman 'uhdah due to ambiguity and validity of daman darak due to custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on daman 'uhdah and daman darak, and explain why one is invalid due to ambiguity and the other valid due to custom.

Core Idea
Daman 'Uhdah is invalid due to ambiguity; Daman Darak is valid due to customary clarity.

In Hanafi fiqh, the validity of a guarantee depends on the clarity of its terms. This lesson examines two types of guarantees related to sales: daman 'uhdah and daman darak. The former is ruled invalid because the term 'uhdah is ambiguous, having multiple possible meanings, while the latter is valid because 'urf (custom) has given it a specific and clear meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two guarantee types
Recognize that the passage discusses Daman 'Uhdah and Daman Darak. Note that both are forms of guarantee (kafalah) related to a sale.
Daman 'Uhdah is also called 'guarantee of title/warranty' and Daman Darak is 'guarantee against claim'.
2
Determine the ruling for each
From the text, Daman 'Uhdah is batil (invalid) and Daman Darak is sahih (valid). Confirm that this is by consensus among the Hanafi school.
The text explicitly states: 'Daman 'Uhdah is invalid by consensus, and Daman Darak is valid by consensus.'
3
Analyze the reason for invalidity of Daman 'Uhdah
The word 'uhdah is ambiguous; it can refer to the old deed, the contract, rights of the slave, darak, or option. Because the meaning is unknown, the guarantee is void.
The ambiguity makes it impossible to determine what exactly is guaranteed, leading to uncertainty (gharar).
4
Analyze the reason for validity of Daman Darak
Although the word darak may have multiple lexical meanings, in common custom it is exclusively used for guarantee against claim (damān istiḥqāq). This clear customary usage removes ambiguity.
Custom ('urf) is a valid source of clarification in Hanafi fiqh.
5
Apply the principle to similar cases
Understand that ambiguity in contract terms can invalidate a contract, while a clear customary meaning can resolve ambiguity. This principle applies to other contexts where terms may be ambiguous.
For example, if a guarantee uses a term that is not commonly understood, it should be clarified or avoided.

And if someone bought a slave, and then a man guaranteed for him regarding the 'uhdah (title/ warranty), then the guarantee is invalid because this word is ambiguous. It may refer to the old deed, which is the property of the seller, so his guarantee is not valid. And it may refer to the contract, to its rights, to the darak, and to the option. Each has a basis, so acting upon it becomes difficult. Unlike the word darak, because it is used customarily for guarantee against claim. Translation: And if someone bought a slave, then a man became guarantor for him regarding the 'uhdah, then this guarantee is invalid because this word is ambiguous. Sometimes the word 'uhdah is used for the old deed, which is the seller's property, so its guarantee is not valid. And sometimes it is used for the contract and its rights, and sometimes for daman bi al-darak, and sometimes for option. Each has a basis, so acting upon it becomes difficult. Contrary to the word darak, because the word darak is used customarily for guarantee against claim. Explanation: There are three types of daman: 1. Daman 'Uhdah, 2. Daman Darak, 3. Daman Khallas. Daman Khallas will be mentioned in the next phrase. Daman 'Uhdah is invalid by consensus, and Daman Darak is valid by consensus. The reason for the invalidity of Daman 'Uhdah is that the meaning of the word 'uhdah is ambiguous and unknown because the word 'uhdah is also used for the old sale deed and old document, and also for the contract, and sometimes for the rights of the slave, and sometimes for darak, and sometimes for option of stipulation. It is used for the old document because the old document is a deed like a written agreement, so this document was named 'uhdah. And it is used for the contract because 'uhdah is derived from 'ahd (covenant), and since 'ahd and 'aqd (contract) are the same, the word 'uhdah is also used for contract. And since the rights of the contract are among its fruits, the word 'uhdah is also used for the rights of the contract. And for the option of stipulation, because there is a hadith: 'The 'uhdah of the companion (slave) is three days', meaning the option of stipulation for the slave is three days. If by the word 'uhdah the old sale deed and document are meant, then the buyer's guarantee from the seller's side regarding this document is invalid because this document is the seller's property and is not a liability upon the seller. Therefore, if this document is destroyed while with the seller, the seller is not liable for it. And it has passed before that whatever is not a liability upon the principal (the guaranteed person), its kafalah and daman are not valid. Therefore, the kafalah of this document will not be valid. Secondly, since this document is the seller's property and in his possession, the guarantor will not be able to hand it over, and since the guarantor's kafalah for something he is unable to do is not valid, therefore the kafalah of the document will also be invalid. In any case, when the meaning of the word 'uhdah is ambiguous and unknown, i.e., the word 'uhdah is used for multiple meanings and it is permissible to interpret it in each meaning, then as long as the guarantor does not clarify his intended meaning, acting upon it will be impossible. So when acting upon it is impossible due to the unknown meaning, then the daman and kafalah along with this word are invalid. And Daman bi al-Darak is valid by consensus because although the word darak is lexically used for multiple meanings, in common custom it is only used for guarantee against claim. That is, the meaning of Daman Darak is that the guarantor said to the buyer: 'If the sold item is claimed, then I am the kafil and guarantor for the return of the price.' So when Daman Darak is customarily used for guarantee against claim, then acting upon it is not impossible, and when acting upon it is not impossible, then Daman bi al-Darak is valid.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (dalil) explaining the validity of two types of guarantee in sales: Daman 'Uhdah and Daman Darak. Daman 'Uhdah refers to a guarantee regarding the 'uhdah (title/deed/warranty) of a purchased slave. The ruling is that it is invalid by consensus. The proof lies in the ambiguity of the word 'uhdah, which can mean five different things: (1) the old deed of sale (which is the seller's property, not a liability), (2) the contract itself, (3) the rights of the contract, (4) darak (claim guarantee), or (5) the option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart) as indicated by the hadith 'uhdat al-rafiq three days. Because the word is ambiguous and the guarantor does not specify which meaning he intends, the guarantee is invalid. Additionally, if it refers to the old deed, the guarantor cannot deliver it as it belongs to the seller, and inability to deliver invalidates the guarantee. In contrast, Daman Darak is valid by consensus. Although the word darak can also have multiple lexical meanings, in common custom ('urf) it is exclusively used for guarantee against claim (i.e., compensation if the sold item is reclaimed). Therefore, there is no ambiguity, and the guarantee is effective. This distinction highlights the Hanafi principle that ambiguity in contract terms can invalidate a contract, while customary usage resolves ambiguity.

Key Terms
عہدہ ('uhdah): A word used in the context of sale guarantees; it is ambiguous and can refer to the old deed, contract, contract rights, darak, or option.
درک (darak): Guarantee against claim (istihqaq); customarily used for the obligation to return the price if the sold item is reclaimed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The validity of a guarantee depends on the clarity of its term; ambiguous terms invalidate, while customary clear terms sustain.

Daman Khulas: The Dispute Between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Presents the intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of dhaman khallas with proofs from each side

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the reasoning behind Abu Hanifa's invalidation and the Sahibayn's validation of Daman Khulas, and identify the operative ruling (mufta bihi).

Core Idea
Daman Khulas: A guarantee to deliver the sold item free of claims—valid if the guarantor can pay value otherwise.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about Daman Khulas, a guarantee where the kafil promises to deliver the sold item free from any claims. Imam Abu Hanifa considers it invalid because absolute delivery is not in the guarantor's power. The Sahibayn analogize it to Daman Darak, allowing the guarantor to pay the value, thus making it valid. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of the conditions for a valid guarantee.

Guided Path
1
Define the issue
Identify what Daman Khulas is: a guarantee to deliver the sold item free from claims. Note that the term 'khulas' implies absolute deliverance.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling and proof
Abu Hanifa holds it invalid (batil). His proof: khulas requires definite delivery, but the guarantor cannot guarantee against third-party claims, making it akin to guaranteeing the impossible.
3
State the Sahibayn's ruling and proof
The Sahibayn hold it valid (sahih). Their proof: khulas is like darak (guarantee of delivery or value). Since darak is valid by consensus, khulas is valid by analogy.
4
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If a person guarantees khulas, according to Abu Hanifa the guarantee is void; according to the Sahibayn it is binding, requiring either delivery of the item or payment of its value.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The Hanafi school adopts the opinion of the Sahibayn, making Daman Khulas valid. This is the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ضمن الخلاص لا يصح عند ابي حنيفة لأنه عبارة عن تخليص المبيع وتسليمه لا محالة وهو غير قادر عليــــه وعـنــدهـما هو بمنزلة الدرك وهو تسليم المبيع أو قيمته فيصح ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے خلاص کی ضمانت کی تو ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک صحیح نہیں ہے کیونکہ مضمان خلاص نام ہے بیع کو خالص کر کے سپرد کرنے کا حالانکہ کفیل اس پر قادر نہیں ہے اور صاحبین کے نزدیک خلاص بمنزلہ درک کے ہے اور وہ بیع سپرد کرنا یا اس کی قیمت سپر د کرنا ہے پس ضمان صحیح ہے۔ تشریح ۔ اس عبارت میں ضمان کی تیسری قسم ضمان خلاص کا بیان ہے۔ ضمان خلاص کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ کفیل نے مشتری سے کہا کہ میں تیرے لئے مبیع کو خلاص کرنے کا ضامن ہوں تو یہ ضمان حضرت امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک صحیح ہے۔ امام صاحب کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ خلاص کا مطلب ہے بیج کو استحقاق وغیرہ سے خالص کر کے سپر د کر نا حالانکہ کفیل حتمی طور سے اس ر قادر نہیں ہے کیونکہ ممکن ہے کہ کوئی شخص اپنا استحقاق ثابت کر کے مبیع کو لے لے پس جب کسی نے اپنا استحقاق ثابت کر کے مبیع کو لے لیا تو لفیل مبیع سپر د کر نے پر قادر نہ رہا اور جب کفیل میچ سپرد کرنے پر قادر نہ رہا تو گویا کفیل نے اپنے اوپر ایسی چیز کو لازم کیا ہے جس کو سپرد کرنے پر قادر نہیں ہے اور ایسی چیز کو لازم کرنا جس کے سپرد کرنے پر قادر نہ ہو باطل ہے اس لئے ضمان خلاص باطل ہے۔ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ خلاص درک کے مرتبہ میں ہے یعنی کفیل نے مشتری سے یہ کہا کہ اگر مبیع سپرد کرنے پر قادر ہوا تو بیع سپردکروں گا اور اگر مبیع سپرد کرنے پر قادر نہ ہوا تو اس کا شمن سپرد کر دوں گا بہر حال ضمان خلاص ، ضمان درک کے مرتبہ میں ہے اور ضمان درک بالا تفاق جائز ہے اس لئے ضمان خلاص بھی جائز ہوگا۔
And if he guarantees the khulas (deliverance), it is not valid according to Abu Hanifa because khulas means definitely delivering the sold item [free from claims], and he is not capable of it. According to them (the two), it is like darak, which is delivering the sold item or its value, so it is valid. Translation: And if someone guaranteed the khulas, then according to Abu Hanifa it is not valid because khulas is the name for freeing the sale and delivering it, whereas the guarantor is not capable of it. And according to the Sahibayn, khulas is like darak, which is delivering the sold item or its price, so the guarantee is valid. Explanation: In this phrase, the third type of daman, Daman Khulas, is mentioned. The meaning of Daman Khulas is that the guarantor said to the buyer: 'I guarantee for you to deliver the sold item [free from claims].' This guarantee, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, is not valid. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that khulas means delivering the sold item free from claims such as istihqaq, and the guarantor is definitely not capable of this because it is possible that someone proves his claim and takes the sold item. Then, when someone proves his claim and takes the sold item, the guarantor is no longer capable of delivering the sold item. And when the guarantor is not capable of delivering, it is as if the guarantor has imposed upon himself something he cannot deliver, and imposing something one cannot deliver is invalid, so Daman Khulas is invalid. The proof of the Sahibayn is that khulas is equivalent to darak. That is, the guarantor said to the buyer: 'If I am able to deliver the sold item, I will deliver it, and if I am not able to deliver it, I will deliver its price.' In any case, Daman Khulas is equivalent to Daman Darak, and Daman Darak is valid by consensus, so Daman Khulas will also be valid.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding Daman Khulas, the third type of guarantee. Daman Khulas is a guarantee by the kafil to deliver the sold item free from claims (e.g., if it is reclaimed by a rightful owner). Imam Abu Hanifa rules that this guarantee is invalid (batil). His reasoning is that khulas requires the guarantor to definitively deliver the item free of all claims, but he is not capable of this because a third party may successfully assert ownership, making delivery impossible. Since the guarantee involves something the guarantor cannot absolutely control, it is akin to guaranteeing the impossible, thus invalid. On the other hand, Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad (the Sahibayn) argue that khulas is like darak: the guarantor's obligation is not absolute delivery but either delivery or payment of the value. In practice, the guarantor promises to deliver the item if possible, otherwise to pay its price. This is a valid guarantee because it is essentially the same as Daman Darak, which is valid by consensus. The operative ruling (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is the opinion of the Sahibayn, as it relies on the analogy to darak and avoids the problem of impossibility. This dispute illustrates the Hanafi principle that a guarantee must involve something the guarantor is capable of fulfilling, but the Sahibayn expand the scope by considering the alternative of paying the value.

Key Terms
خلاص (khulas): Guarantee of deliverance; the guarantor's promise to deliver the sold item free from claims or to pay its value.
صاحبین (Sahibayn): The two companions of Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
درك (darak): Guarantee of the sold item's safety; the guarantor promises to deliver the item or its value if it is lost or claimed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Abu Hanifa rule that Daman Khulas is invalid?
A) Because it contradicts the Quran
B) Because the guarantor cannot guarantee delivery of the specific item free of claims absolutely
C) Because it is not mentioned in the books of fiqh
D) Because it requires a formal contract

Key Takeaway
Daman Khulas is valid according to the Sahibayn; the operative Hanafi ruling allows the guarantor to either deliver the item or pay its value.

Kafālat al-Khalāṣ: The Dispute on Guaranteeing Release
Explains dispute on validity of guaranteeing the release of a sold item.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abū Ḥanīfah and the ṣāḥibayn on kafālat al-khalāṣ, explain the reasoning behind each, and identify the muftā bihi position.

Core Idea
A guarantee to free a sold item is valid by analogy to the universally accepted ḍamān al-darak.

This passage addresses a nuanced intra-Ḥanafī dispute over a specific type of guarantee known as kafālat al-khalāṣ (guarantee of release). The disagreement centers on whether the guarantor's obligation is possible or impossible. We will examine the proofs of Imām Abū Ḥanīfah and the two disciples (Ṣāḥibayn) and see how the later school settled on the view that validates this contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract
Define kafālat al-khalāṣ: the guarantor promises to free the sold item from any legal claim and deliver it to the buyer. Understand why this guarantee is needed in sales where third-party claims may arise.
Distinguish from ḍamān al-darak, which is a guarantee to compensate if the item is taken, not necessarily to deliver it free of claims.
2
Extract Abū Ḥanīfah’s position
State his ruling: invalid (bāṭil). Identify his core argument: khalāṣ means absolute delivery, which the guarantor cannot guarantee because a rightful owner may take the item. Obligating the impossible is invalid.
Abū Ḥanīfah focuses on the literal impossibility of delivering the item free of all claims.
3
Extract the ṣāḥibayn’s position
State their ruling: valid (ṣaḥīḥ). Identify their analogical argument: khalāṣ is like darak; the guarantor commits to either deliver the item or pay its value. This is possible, so the guarantee is valid.
The ṣāḥibayn reinterpret khalāṣ to include a compensatory alternative, making the obligation feasible.
4
Determine the operative ruling
Identify the muftā bihi position: the ṣāḥibayn’s view is relied upon in fatwa. Explain why: it aligns with the consensus on ḍamān al-darak and is more practical. Note the thamarah al-khilaf: under Abū Ḥanīfah the guarantor has no liability; under the ṣāḥibayn he must pay the price if unable to deliver.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school, and the later tarjīh favors the ṣāḥibayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خلاصی کی ضمانت کا حکم
ولو ضمن الخلاص لا يصح عند ابي حنيفة لأنه عبارة عن تخليص المبيع وتسليمه لا محالة وهو غير قادر عليــــه وعـنــدهـما هو بمنزلة الدرك وهو تسليم المبيع أو قيمته فيصح
ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے خلاص کی ضمانت کی تو ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک صحیح نہیں ہے کیونکہ مضمان خلاص نام ہے بیع کو خالص کر کے سپرد کرنے کا حالانکہ کفیل اس پر قادر نہیں ہے اور صاحبین کے نزدیک خلاص بمنزلہ درک کے ہے اور وہ بیع سپرد کرنا یا اس کی قیمت سپر د کرنا ہے پس ضمان صحیح ہے۔ تشریح ۔ اس عبارت میں ضمان کی تیسری قسم ضمان خلاص کا بیان ہے۔ ضمان خلاص کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ کفیل نے مشتری سے کہا کہ میں تیرے لئے مبیع کو خلاص کرنے کا ضامن ہوں تو یہ ضمان حضرت امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک صحیح ہے۔
امام صاحب کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ خلاص کا مطلب ہے بیج کو استحقاق وغیرہ سے خالص کر کے سپر د کر نا حالانکہ کفیل حتمی طور سے اس ر قادر نہیں ہے کیونکہ ممکن ہے کہ کوئی شخص اپنا استحقاق ثابت کر کے مبیع کو لے لے پس جب کسی نے اپنا استحقاق ثابت کر کے مبیع کو لے لیا تو لفیل مبیع سپر د کر نے پر قادر نہ رہا اور جب کفیل میچ سپرد کرنے پر قادر نہ رہا تو گویا کفیل نے اپنے اوپر ایسی چیز کو لازم کیا ہے جس کو سپرد کرنے پر قادر نہیں ہے اور ایسی چیز کو لازم کرنا جس کے سپرد کرنے پر قادر نہ ہو باطل ہے اس لئے ضمان خلاص باطل ہے۔ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ خلاص درک کے مرتبہ میں ہے یعنی کفیل نے مشتری سے یہ کہا کہ اگر مبیع سپرد کرنے پر قادر ہوا تو بیع سپردکروں گا اور اگر مبیع سپرد کرنے پر قادر نہ ہوا تو اس کا شمن سپرد کر دوں گا بہر حال ضمان خلاص ، ضمان درک کے مرتبہ میں ہے اور ضمان درک بالا تفاق جائز ہے اس لئے ضمان خلاص بھی جائز ہوگا۔
Ruling on guarantee of release: And if he guarantees the release, it is not valid according to Abu Hanifa because it means delivering the sold item free of claims, which he is unable to do. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is like the guarantee of defect (darak), which is delivering the item or its value, so it is valid. Explanation: In this phrase, the third type of guarantee, kafalat al-khalas, is mentioned. The meaning is that the guarantor says to the buyer: 'I guarantee to free the sold item for you.' According to Imam Abu Hanifa, this guarantee is not valid. Imam's proof: release means to free the item from claims and deliver it, and the guarantor is definitively unable to do so because someone may prove their claim and take the item. If that happens, the guarantor cannot deliver the item, so he has obligated himself to something he cannot deliver, which is invalid. The proof of the two (Sahibayn): release is like darak, i.e., the guarantor says: 'If I can deliver the item, I will; if not, I will pay its value.' So it is like the guarantee of darak, which is unanimously valid, so kafalat al-khalas is also valid.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage discusses the third type of guarantee (kafālat al-khalāṣ) and presents an intra-Ḥanafī dispute between Imām Abū Ḥanīfah and the ṣāḥibayn (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad al-Shaybānī). The term khalāṣ refers to freeing a sold item from any legal claim (e.g., a rightful owner) and delivering it to the buyer. Abū Ḥanīfah argues that the guarantor cannot guarantee such an outcome because he has no control over whether a third party will successfully claim the item; if the item is taken by a rightful owner, the guarantor cannot deliver it. Obligating oneself to an impossible action renders the guarantee invalid (bāṭil). The ṣāḥibayn, however, draw an analogy to ḍamān al-darak (guarantee against defects of title), which is valid by consensus. They interpret khalāṣ as a commitment to either deliver the item or pay its price, thus making the obligation possible. The relied-upon (muftā bihi) position is that of the ṣāḥibayn, as it aligns with the general acceptance of ḍamān al-darak. This dispute highlights the Ḥanafī method of qiyās and the consideration of actual capability in guarantees (kafālah).

Key Terms
كفالة الخلاص (kafālat al-khalāṣ): A guarantee where the guarantor promises to free a sold item from any legal claim and deliver it to the buyer.
ضمان الدرك (ḍamān al-darak): A guarantee that if the sold item is taken by a rightful owner, the guarantor will refund the price or compensate.
خلاص (khalāṣ): Literal: freeing/releasing. Technically: freeing the sold item from any third-party claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the muftā bihi ruling on kafālat al-khalāṣ in the Ḥanafī school?
A) Invalid because it involves an impossible obligation.
B) Valid only if written down.
C) Valid by analogy to ḍamān al-darak.
D) Invalid unless the guarantor is the seller.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, kafālat al-khalāṣ is valid (muftā bihi) by analogy to ḍamān al-darak.

Mutual Guarantee and Recourse for Excess Over Half
Ruling on recourse when two mutually guaranteeing debtors pay more than half.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on recourse when two joint debtors mutually guarantee each other and one pays more than half, and explain the reasoning based on asil/kafil roles and avoidance of dawr.

Core Idea
When two debtors mutually guarantee each other, each pays his own half first; only the excess over half is recoverable as a guarantor.

This passage introduces the chapter on kafalah involving two persons. It presents a nuanced ruling: if two people jointly purchase a slave for 1000 dirhams and each guarantees the other, the one who pays the entire debt can only recover the amount exceeding half. The reasoning hinges on each being principal (asil) for half and guarantor (kafil) for the other half, and the need to avoid circularity (dawr). Let's break down the logic behind this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and transaction
Two persons (Zayd and Amr) jointly buy a slave for 1000 dirhams. Each becomes a guarantor for the other's half of the debt.
The debt is joint, and the guarantee is mutual.
2
State the core question
If Zayd pays the full 1000 dirhams, how much can he recover from Amr?
Intuitively, one might think he can recover 500 (Amr's share), but the ruling limits recovery.
3
Apply the asil/kafil classification
Each debtor is principal (asil) for his own half (500 dirhams) and guarantor (kafil) for the other's half. The payment first satisfies his own principal liability.
The first 500 dirhams paid is Zayd's own debt; the remaining 500 is paid as Amr's guarantor.
4
Analyze the excess over half
Only the amount exceeding half of the total debt (i.e., 500 dirhams in this case) is considered payment as guarantor; thus Zayd can recover only that excess from Amr.
If Zayd pays 600 dirhams, he can recover 100 dirhams (600 - 500).
5
Recognize the avoidance of circularity (dawr)
If the payment were attributed to the partner's half directly, the partner could then also claim back from the payer because the payer's payment is considered the partner's payment, leading to an endless cycle.
The principle of dawr prevents this logical impossibility.

Source Text
