Two Mukatab Slaves as Sureties: Hanafi Istihsan vs Qiyas
Explanation of Hanafi ruling on two jointly contracted mukatib slaves suretyship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi legal construction that validates two mukatab slaves being sureties for each other and evaluate the opposing school's qiyas-based objection.

Core Idea
Istihsan recharacterizes the arrangement: each slave is principal for the full sum, not a surety, avoiding prohibitions.

This lesson examines a specific case from al-Hidayah where two slaves are jointly contracted as mukatib and each stands surety for the other. The Hanafi school uses istihsan (juristic preference) to validate this, while other schools reject it by qiyas (analogy). Understanding this construction reveals how Hanafi jurists creatively preserve contractual intent within the boundaries of fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core problem
Two prohibitions are at play: (1) a mukatab cannot be a surety because he cannot make gratuitous transfers; (2) suretyship for badal al-kitabah (kitabah payment) is invalid because it is not a valid debt. How does the Hanafi school avoid both?
Focus on the legal construction used.
2
Understand the Hanafi construction
The Hanafi jurists treat each slave as a principal debtor (asl) for the full 1,000 dirhams, and their emancipation is conditioned on paying the full amount. Thus, the arrangement is not a suretyship at all.
This recharacterization avoids both prohibitions because no suretyship by a mukatab and no suretyship for kitabah payment.
3
Analyze the consequences of payment
If one slave pays the full 1,000, both are freed. The paying slave recovers half from his companion to maintain equality, as each was originally obligated for the full amount in the construction.
The equality is derived from the legal fiction that each was a principal for the whole.
4
Examine the scenario where the master frees one slave
If the master frees one slave before any payment, the freed slave is liable for half the sum (1,000) and the other remains liable for half. The master can collect from either: from the freed slave as surety (since he was made a surety under the construction) or from the other as principal.
The freed slave, if he pays, can recover from the other; the other cannot recover from him.
5
Evaluate the cross-madhhab dispute
Imam Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad follow qiyas: they see a mukatab acting as surety and suretyship for kitabah payment, both prohibited, so they reject the arrangement entirely. The Hanafi response is that their own construction removes the basis for the prohibition.
The practical difference: under Hanafi law, the arrangement is valid; under others, each slave is only responsible for his own half.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الالف عليه فيكون عتقهما معلقا بأدائه ويجعل كفيلا بالالف في حق صاحبه وسنذكره في المكاتب ان شاء الله تعالى واذا عرف ذلك فما اداه احدهما رجع بنصفه على صاحبه لاستوائهما ولو رجع بالكل لا تتحقق المساواة

قال ولو لم يؤديا شيئا حتى اعتق المولى احدهما جاز العتق لمصادفته ملكه وبرئ عن النصف لانه ما رضي بالتزام المال الا ليكون المال وسيلة الى العتق وما بقي وسيلة فيسقط ويبقى النصف على الآخر لان المال في الحقيقة مقابل برقبتهما وانما جعل على كل واحد منهما احتياطا لتصحيح الزمان واذا جاء العتق استغنى عنه فاعتبر مقابلا برقبتهما فلهذا ينتصف وللمولى ان ياخذ بحصة الذي لم يعتق ايهما شاء المعتق بالكفالة وصاحبه بالأصالة فان اخذ الذي اعتق رجع على صاحبه بما يؤدي لانه مؤد عنه بامره وان اخذ الآخر لم يرجع على المعتق بشئ لانه أدى عن نفسه والله أعلم
The thousand is upon him, so their emancipation is conditioned upon its payment, and he is made a surety for the thousand regarding the rights of his companion. We will mention this in the chapter of Mukatab, God willing. And when that is understood, whatever one of them pays, he may reclaim half from his companion, by virtue of their equality; if he reclaims the whole, equality is not realized.

He said: If they have not paid anything until the master frees one of them, the emancipation is valid because it coincides with his property, and he is free from half, because he only agreed to undertake the payment so that the payment would be a means to emancipation, and the means has ceased, so it falls. And half remains upon the other, because the payment is in reality in exchange for both their persons, and it was made obligatory upon each of them only as a precaution to validate the time. And when emancipation occurs, the need for it is dispensed with, so it is considered in exchange for both their persons; therefore it becomes half. And the master may take the portion of the one who was not emancipated from whichever he wishes: from the freed one by virtue of suretyship, and from his companion by virtue of being a principal. If he takes from the freed one, he may reclaim from his companion what he pays, because he pays on his behalf by his order. And if he takes from the other, he does not reclaim anything from the freed one, because he pays for himself. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from al-Hidayah, expanded by later commentary, on a specific case of suretyship (kafalah) in the context of kitabah (contractual manumission). The issue: two slaves are jointly made mukatib under a single contract for 1,000 dirhams, and each becomes surety for the other. The Hanafi school, using istihsan (juristic preference), validates this arrangement, while other schools (Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali) reject it based on qiyas (analogy). The Hanafi reasoning employs a legal construction: each slave is treated as if the full 1,000 is due from him directly (as asl – principal), not as a surety, and their freedom is suspended on payment of the full amount. This avoids the prohibition against a mukatab acting as surety (since he cannot make gratuitous transfers) and against suretyship for badal al-kitabah (which is not a valid debt). The construction makes the payment a condition for freedom rather than a true kitabah price. Consequently, if one slave pays any amount, he can reclaim half from his companion to maintain equality. The passage also discusses the scenario where the master frees one slave before any payment: the freed slave is liable for half the sum (since his original obligation was for the entire 1,000 for the sake of validation, but now the need for that construction is gone, so the actual equal share applies), and the master can claim from either the freed slave (as surety) or the remaining slave (as principal). If the master takes from the freed slave, that slave can recover from the remaining slave; if from the remaining slave, he has no recourse against the freed slave.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): A contract of guarantee or suretyship, where a person (kafil) becomes responsible for a debt or obligation of another (asil) to the creditor.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract (kitabah) with his master to earn his freedom by paying a specified sum in installments.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference used to depart from a strict qiyas (analogy) in favor of a more equitable solution, often based on textual evidence or custom.
بدل الكتابة (badal al-kitabah): The payment (price) agreed upon in the kitabah contract for the slave's freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi position on two jointly contracted mukatib slaves each being surety for the other, what legal construction validates the arrangement?
A) Each slave is treated as a surety only for his own share (500 dirhams) and the suretyship is for the other's share.
B) Each slave is treated as a principal debtor for the full 1,000 dirhams, and their freedom is conditioned on payment of the full amount.
C) The suretyship is considered a separate contract independent of the kitabah.
D) The master is considered to have waived the prohibition against a mukatab being a surety.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi validation of suretyship between jointly contracted mukatab slaves relies on istihsan to recharacterize the arrangement as a conditional principal obligation, avoiding qiyas-based prohibitions.

Freeing One of Two Joint Mukatib Slaves Before Payment
Ruling and reasoning when master frees one joint mukatib before any payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when the master frees one of two joint mukatib slaves before any payment, explain the rationale based on the debt as a means to freedom and the guarantee structure, and apply the ruling to similar scenarios.

Core Idea
Freeing one joint mukatib before payment extinguishes his half of the debt; the other slave remains liable for the remaining half.

When two slaves enter a joint kitabah contract with mutual guarantee, each slave's debt is a means to freedom. If the master frees one slave before any payment, the purpose of the debt for that slave is fulfilled, so half the total debt is waived. The remaining half is owed by the other slave, whom the master may claim from either slave due to the guarantee structure, with specific recourse rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
Two slaves jointly mukatab with mutual guarantee. They have paid nothing. Master frees one. The freeing is valid, and the freed slave is free from half the kitabah debt.
The ruling hinges on the debt being a means to freedom.
2
Understand the rationale: debt as a device (hila)
The full debt was initially made incumbent on each slave fully only as a device to make the guarantee valid. When freedom comes through manumission, that device is no longer needed, so the debt is re-categorized as in exchange for both necks, hence halved.
This illustrates Hanafi use of legal devices (hiyal) to achieve substantive justice.
3
Analyze the guarantee (kafalah) relationships
Each slave is both principal debtor for his own share and guarantor for the other's share. The master can claim the remaining half from either slave: from the freed slave as guarantor, or from the unfreed slave as principal.
The freed slave pays as a guarantor if the master claims from him.
4
Determine recourse rights
If the master takes from the freed slave (guarantor), the freed slave can seek recourse from the unfreed slave because he paid on his behalf. If the master takes from the unfreed slave (principal), that slave cannot seek recourse from the freed slave because he paid his own debt.
Recourse based on who is the ultimate obligor.
5
Apply the ruling to analogous cases
Consider if only one slave is freed after partial payment: the proportion would adjust accordingly. The principle is that freeing a slave extinguishes the debt to the extent of his ownership share.
The ruling is specific to joint kitabah with mutual guarantee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ولو لم يؤديا شيئا حتى اعتق المولى احدهما جاز العتق لمصادفته ملكه وبرى عن النصف لانه مارضى بالتزام المال الاليكون المال وسيلة الى العتق وما بقى وسيلة فيسقط ويبقى النصف على الاخر لان المال في الحقيقة مقابل برقبتهما وانما جعل على كل واحد منهما احتيا لا لتصحيح الزمان واذا جاء العتق استغنى عنه فاعتبر مقابلا برقبتهما فلهذا ينتصف وللمولى ان ياخذ بحصة الذى لم يعتق ايهما شاء المعتق بالكفالة وصاحبه بالأصالة فان اخذ الذى اعتق رجع على صاحبه بما يؤدى لانه مؤد عنه بامره وان احد الاخر لم يرجع على المعتق بشئ لانه اذى عن نفسه والله أعلم
He said: And if they did not pay anything until the master freed one of them, the freeing is valid because it reaches his property, and he is free from half, because he (the slave) did not agree to take on the debt except that the money would be a means to freedom, and when the means is no longer present, it falls. The half remains on the other, because in reality the money is in exchange for their necks, and it was made incumbent on each of them only as a device to correct the time, and when freedom comes, there is no need for that device, so it is considered in exchange for their necks, thus it is halved. And the master has the right to take the share of the one who was not freed from whichever of them he wishes: from the freed slave by virtue of guarantee, and from his companion by virtue of principal. So if he takes from the one who was freed, he (the freed slave) seeks recourse from his companion for what he paid, because he paid on his behalf by his command. And if he takes from the other, he does not seek recourse from the freed slave for anything, because he paid on behalf of himself. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hidayah regarding the joint kitabah of two slaves with mutual guarantee. The scenario: two slaves are made mukatib jointly with a single payment of 1000 dirhams, and each is the guarantor (kafeel) for the other. They have not paid anything, and then the master frees one of them. The ruling: the freeing is valid because the slave was still owned (since kitabah debt was unpaid). The freed slave is absolved of half the kitabah debt. Why? Because he only agreed to the debt as a means to freedom; once freedom is attained via manumission, the purpose is fulfilled, so the debt falls away. The remaining half of the debt stays on the other slave. In reality, the full debt is in exchange for both slaves' necks, but initially it was made incumbent on each fully only as a device (hila) to make the guarantee valid. When full freedom occurs, that device is no longer needed, so the debt is considered as being for both, thus halved. The master may claim the half share from either the freed slave (by virtue of guarantee) or from the unfreed slave (by virtue of principal obligation). If the master claims from the freed slave, the freed slave pays and then seeks recourse from the unfreed slave, because he paid by his command (as a guarantor). If the master claims from the unfreed slave, that slave pays from his own obligation and cannot seek recourse from the freed slave. This ruling illustrates the Hanafi approach of using legal devices (hiyal) to achieve valid outcomes while preserving substantive principles.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafālah): Guarantee or surety; here, each slave becomes surety for the other's payment of the kitabah debt.
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has entered into a contract of kitabah with the master to earn and pay a sum for freedom.
بدل الكتابة (badal al-kitābah): The ransom price agreed upon for the slave's freedom under kitabah.
كفيل (kafīl): The guarantor; in this context, each slave is kafīl for the other's payment.
مكفول عنه (makfūl ‘anhu): The principal debtor or the one for whom guarantee is given; here, the other slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where the master frees one of two joint mukatib slaves before any payment, which of the following is true?
A) The freed slave is free and owes nothing; the other slave owes the full original debt.
B) The freed slave is free and owes nothing; the other slave owes half the original debt.
C) Both slaves remain obligated for the full debt despite the freeing.
D) The master cannot free a single slave; both must be freed together.

Key Takeaway
Freeing one joint mukatib before payment halves the debt: the freed slave is free from half, and the other slave must pay the remaining half, with the master having collection and recourse rights under the guarantee.

Joint Mukatab Slaves with Mutual Kafalah: Effect of Manumission of One
Ruling with proof via istihsan on joint mukatab kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when one of two joint mukatab guarantors is freed and explain the istihsan basis for the discharge of half the kitabah debt.

Core Idea
When freedom is attained directly, the means (kitabah payment) falls away, and the debt is halved.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah explores a nuanced scenario in the Book of Kafalah: two slaves jointly mukatab under a single contract, each guaranteeing the other's payment. If the master frees one before any payment, what happens to the kitabah debt? The Hanafi ruling, based on istihsan, provides a balanced solution that respects both the master's ownership and the slave's freedom. Understanding this ruling clarifies the role of legal devices (hiyal) in facilitating permissible outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and preconditions
Note the specific conditions: two slaves are jointly mukatab under a single kitabah contract, each has become a guarantor (kafil) for the other's share of the kitabah payment, and neither has paid anything yet.
The ruling only applies if these conditions are met exactly.
2
State the ruling
The master's freeing of one slave is valid (sahih) and effective. The freed slave is discharged from half the kitabah debt. The other slave remains liable for the remaining half.
This is the central hukm: freeing is permissible and has these financial consequences.
3
Trace the reasoning (istihsan)
The reasoning is based on istihsan: the kitabah money each slave undertook was not a true debt but a means (wasila) to achieve freedom. When freedom is granted directly by the master, the means becomes unnecessary, so half the payment falls away. This overrides the general rule that kitabah debt is property subject to kafalah.
The key is that the money's purpose was as a means, not an end.
4
Examine the master's options and recourse
The master may demand the remaining half from either slave: from the freed one due to his kafalah (guarantee), or from the other due to original liability. If the freed guarantor pays, he can recover from the other slave. If the other pays, he has no recourse against the freed one.
The master's right is flexible; recourse depends on who pays.
5
Address the objection
An objection arises: kafalah of kitabah debt is generally invalid, yet here the freed slave is held as guarantor. The answer distinguishes that this money is not truly badal kitabah but a condition upon which freedom was suspended; thus its kafalah is valid. This follows the principle that continuity follows establishment (al-baqa' yuwafi al-thubut).
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر دو غلاموں کو کتابت واحدہ کے تحت مکاتب کیا گیا اور دونوں میں سے ہر ایک اپنے ساتھی کی طرف سے کفیل ہو گیا مگر ابھی تک دونوں میں سے کسی نے کچھ ادا نہیں کیا یہاں تک کہ مولی نے یہاں یہ فرض کیا گیا ہے کہ ابھی تک کچھ ادا نہیں کیا ہے۔ پس جب مکاتب نے بدل کتابت کا کچھ حصہ ادا نہیں کیا تو وہ مملوک ہوگا اور مملوک کو آزاد کرنا چونکہ جائز ہے۔ اس لئے اس کو آزاد کرنا بھی جائز اور نافذ ہوگئی ہے اس لئے مال کا وسیلہ آزاد ہونا باقی نہ رہا، اور جب مال کا وسیلہ ہونا باقی نہ رہا تو بدل کتابت میں سے نصف ساقط ہو گیا اور ایک نصف دوسرے مکاتب پر باقی رہا۔ کیونکہ مال حقیقت میں دونوں مکاتبوں کی آزادی کا عوض ہے اور سابقہ مسئلہ میں ہر ایک پر پورا پورا بدل کتابت واجب کرنا کفالہ کے صحیح کرنے کے لئے محض ایک حیلہ تھا مگر جب ایک مکاتب آزاد ہو گیا تو اس حیلہ کی چنداں ضرورت نہ رہی اور اس مال کتابت کو دونوں مکاتبوں کی آزادی کا مقابل ٹھہرا گر آزاد شدہ مکاتب کو نصف بدل کتابت سے بری کر دیا گیا۔ اور دوسرے مکاتب پر نصف بدل کتابت باقی رکھا گیا اور اس نصف بدل کتابت کے بارے میں مولی کو اختیار ہے کہ وہ اس کا مطالبہ آزاد شدہ مکاتب سے کرے یا غیر آزاد شدہ مکاتب سے کرے۔ آزاد شدہ مکاتب سے مطالبہ کا حق کفالہ کی وجہ سے ہوگا اور دوسرے سے مطالبہ کا حق اس لئے ہوگا کہ اُس پر اصلا واجب ہے۔ اب اگر آزاد شده مکاتب نے ادا کیا تو وہ اپنے ساتھی سے واپس لے گا۔ کیونکہ اُس نے اس کے حکم سے ادا کیا ہے۔ اور کفیل جب مکفول عنہ کے حکم سے ادا کرتا ہے تو کفیل کو مکفول عنہ سے رجوع کرنے کا اختیار ہوتا ہے۔ اور اگر خود غیر آزاد شدہ مکاتب نے ادا کیا ہو تو اس کو آزاد شدہ سے واپس لینے کا اختیار نہ ہو گا۔ کیونکہ اس نے جو کچھ ادا کیا اپنی طرف سے ادا کیا ہے۔ اور اپنی طرف سے ادا کرنے والے کو رجوع کا اختیار نہیں ہوتا ۔ لہذا اس کو بھی رجوع کا اختیار نہ ہوگا۔ واللہ اعلم بالصواب۔
اشکال یہاں ایک اشکال ہے۔ وہ یہ کہ مولی جب آزاد شدہ مکاتب سے مطالبہ کرے گا تو اُس سے کفیل ہونے کی وجہ سے مطالبہ کیا جائے گا۔ اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ بدل کتابت کا کفالہ صحیح ہے حالانکہ بدل کتابت کا کفالہ باطل ہے؟ جواب یہ ہے کہ سابقہ مسئلہ میں گذر چکا کہ ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک پر پورے مال کا مطالبہ واجب تھا مگر جب نصف ساقط ہو کر نصف رہ گیا تو یہ اُسی صفت پر رہے گا جس پر ثابت ہوا تھا۔ کیونکہ بقا ثبوت کے موافق ہوتی ہے۔ اور سابق میں گذر چکا کہ یہ مال، بدل کتابت نہیں ہے بلکہ اس کی ادائیگی پر آزادی کو معلق کیا گیا ہے۔ اور یہ مال جب بدل کتابت نہیں ہے تو اس کا کفالہ بھی درست ہوگا۔
Book of Kafalah. And if both have not paid anything until the master freed one of them, then freeing is permissible because freedom has reached the master's ownership, and that slave is cleared of half the kitabah debt because the slave who was freed was not consenting to making the money obligatory except so that the money would be a means to freedom. And since the money does not remain a means, the money falls away. And the other remains liable for half because the money is in reality the equivalent of the two slaves, and it was fixed upon each of them only as a device (hila) to make the kafalah valid. When freedom came, the device became unnecessary. So that money is considered as the equivalent of the two slaves, and therefore it was divided into halves. And the master has the option to take the share of the slave who was not freed from whichever of the two he wishes: from the freed one due to kafalah, and from his companion due to original liability. Then if the master took from the one who was freed, he will recover from his companion what he paid because he paid on his behalf by his order. And if he took from the other, he will not take anything from the freed one because he paid on his own behalf. Tashrih: The scenario of the issue is that if two slaves were made mukatab under a single kitabah contract, and each of them became a guarantor for his companion, but neither had paid anything yet, until the master freed one of them, then this freeing is permissible because the master freed the slave while he was his property, because as long as any part of the kitabah payment remains on the mukatab, he remains the master's property. Here it is assumed that nothing has been paid yet. So when the mukatab has not paid any part of the kitabah payment, he is property, and freeing a property is permissible, so his freeing is also permissible and effective. When this mukatab becomes free, he is cleared of half the kitabah payment because he had made the money obligatory upon himself only so that this money would be a means and instrument for freedom. But since freedom has been obtained through another means, the money as a means no longer remains. When the money's being a means no longer remains, half of the kitabah payment falls away, and one half remains on the other mukatab because the money is in reality the price of freedom of both mukatabs, and in the previous issue, making the full kitabah payment obligatory on each was merely a device (hila) to validate the kafalah. But when one mukatab became free, there was no great need for that device, and that kitabah money is considered the equivalent of the freedom of both mukatabs, so the freed mukatab is cleared of half the kitabah payment, and half the kitabah payment remains on the other mukatab. Regarding this half kitabah payment, the master has the option to demand it from the freed mukatab or from the unfreed mukatab. The right to demand from the freed mukatab is due to kafalah, and from the other is because it is originally obligatory on him. Now if the freed mukatab pays, he will recover from his companion because he paid by his order, and when a guarantor pays by the order of the principal, the guarantor has the right to recourse against the principal. And if the unfreed mukatab himself paid, he has no right to recover from the freed one because what he paid he paid on his own behalf, and one who pays on his own behalf has no right of recourse. So he will have no right of recourse. And Allah knows best what is correct. Objection: There is an objection here, namely that when the master demands from the freed mukatab, he demands from him due to kafalah. This indicates that kafalah of kitabah payment is valid, whereas kafalah of kitabah payment is invalid. The answer is that in the previous issue it has passed that on each of them the demand of the full money was obligatory, but when half fell away and half remained, it remains on the same description upon which it was established, because continuity is in accordance with establishment. And previously it has passed that this money is not the kitabah payment, rather freedom is suspended upon its payment. And when this money is not the kitabah payment, its kafalah is also valid.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah explains a specific ruling in the Book of Kafalah regarding joint mukatab slaves who guarantee each other's kitabah payment. The author first states the ruling: if two slaves are jointly mukatab (both are under a single kitabah contract), and each becomes a guarantor (kafil) for the other's share, and the master frees one of them before any payment, the freeing is valid. The freed slave becomes free and is discharged from half the kitabah debt. The other slave remains liable for the other half. The master may demand this half from either slave: from the freed one as guarantor, or from the other as the original debtor. If the freed guarantor pays, he may recover from the other slave; if the other pays, he has no recourse. The author then provides a detailed explanation (tashrih) that confirms the ruling and elaborates the reasoning. The key principle is that the money each slave undertook was not truly badal kitabah but a device (hila) to make the kafalah valid. Since freedom came directly from the master, the device is no longer needed, and the money is treated as the price of freedom for both, so it is halved. The passage then addresses an objection: if kafalah of kitabah debt is generally invalid, how can the freed slave be held as guarantor? The answer distinguishes that this money is not actually kitabah payment (badal kitabah) but a condition upon which freedom was suspended, so its kafalah is valid. This follows the Hanafi principle that continuity follows establishment (al-baqa' yuwafi al-thubut).

Key Terms
مکاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a kitabah contract with his master to purchase his freedom by paying installments.
کتابت (kitabah): The contract between master and slave where the slave agrees to pay a sum for freedom.
کفالہ (kafalah): Suretyship; a contract where one person (kafil) guarantees the debt or obligation of another (makful 'anhu) to a third party (makful lahu).
بدل کتابت (badal kitabah): The payment due under the kitabah contract; the sum the slave must pay to become free.
حیلہ (hila): A legal device or stratagem used to achieve a lawful result while avoiding a prohibition or fulfilling a condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of two joint mukatab slaves who are each other's guarantors, if the master frees one before any payment is made, what happens to the kitabah debt?
A) The entire debt is cancelled and both slaves become free.
B) The freed slave remains liable for the entire debt as guarantor.
C) Half the debt is cancelled, and the other slave is liable for the remaining half.
D) The debt remains unchanged and the freed slave must pay his half.

Key Takeaway
In joint mukatab with mutual kafalah, freeing one slave discharges half the debt; the master has recourse against either, with the freed guarantor having recourse against the other.

Kafalah for Badal Kitabah: Valid Despite Apparent Contradiction
Resolves contradiction and explains validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between badal kitabah and a conditional payment for emancipation, and state when kafalah is valid in such contexts.

Core Idea
Kafalah for a conditional payment is valid even if kafalah for badal kitabah is invalid.

This passage clarifies a seeming contradiction: although kafalah for badal kitabah is generally invalid, a previous ruling declared it valid. The author resolves this by distinguishing between the actual badal kitabah and a separate sum that was made a condition for emancipation. The legal principle 'al-baqa' ala al-thubut' (remaining follows establishment) ensures the ruling matches the original nature of the debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contradiction
Note that kafalah for badal kitabah is usually invalid, yet this passage says it is valid. Find the resolution in the text.
2
Distinguish the nature of the debt
The wealth in question is not the actual badal kitabah but a sum that was made a condition for emancipation. Recognize the difference.
The text explicitly says 'this wealth is not badal kitabah'.
3
Apply the principle of continuity
The ruling must remain consistent with the original establishment. Since the debt was not established as badal kitabah, its kafalah follows a different rule.
The principle 'al-baqa' ala al-thubut' is invoked.
4
Conclude the validity
Because the debt is not badal kitabah, kafalah for it is valid. State the final ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ بدل کتابت کا کفالہ صحیح ہے حالانکہ بدل کتابت کا کفالہ باطل ہے؟ جواب یہ ہے کہ سابقہ مسئلہ میں گذر چکا کہ ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک پر پورے مال کا مطالبہ واجب تھا مگر جب نصف ساقط ہو کر نصف رہ گیا تو یہ اُسی صفت پر رہے گا جس پر ثابت ہوا تھا۔ کیونکہ بقا ثبوت کے موافق ہوتی ہے۔ اور سابق میں گذر چکا کہ یہ مال، بدل کتابت نہیں ہے بلکہ اس کی ادائیگی پر آزادی کو معلق کیا گیا ہے۔ اور یہ مال جب بدل کتابت نہیں ہے تو اس کا کفالہ بھی درست ہوگا۔ اللهم اغفر لي ولوالدي و لكاتبه
From this it is known that the kafalah of badal kitabah is valid, although the kafalah of badal kitabah is invalid? The answer is that in the previous issue it has already passed that from each of these two, the demand of the full wealth was obligatory, but when half fell and half remained, then it will remain on the same description upon which it was established. Because remaining is according to establishment. And previously it has passed that this wealth is not badal kitabah, but rather emancipation is made conditional upon its payment. And when this wealth is not badal kitabah, then its kafalah will also be correct. O Allah, forgive me, my parents, and its scribe.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory note addressing a potential contradiction. The author, commenting on a previous ruling, states that kafalah for badal kitabah (the payment for a kitabah contract) appears to be invalid, yet here it is considered valid. The resolution: the mal (wealth) in question is not the actual badal kitabah but a separate sum that was made a condition for emancipation. Since the nature of the debt is different, the ruling for kafalah follows accordingly. The author follows the order of the source text, clarifying that the validity of the kafalah depends on the nature of the underlying obligation.

Key Terms
بدل کتابت (badal al-kitabah): The payment agreed upon in a kitabah contract for the slave's emancipation.
کفالہ (kafalah): A contract of suretyship where a person (kafil) guarantees the debt or presence of another.
مال (mal): Wealth or property; in this context, a sum of money owed by the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is kafalah for badal kitabah considered valid in this passage despite it usually being invalid?
A) Because the slave is free.
B) Because the wealth is not actually badal kitabah but a condition for emancipation.
C) Because the kafil is wealthy.
D) Because the debt is deferred.

Key Takeaway
Kafalah for a debt related to kitabah is valid only if the debt is not the actual badal kitabah but a separate conditional payment.

Kafalah on Behalf of a Slave: Ruling When Debt is Present but Not Collectible
Introduces rulings for kafalah involving a slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a kafalah on behalf of a slave when the debt is due but not immediately collectible, and explain the reasoning based on the slave's lack of capacity.

Core Idea
Kafalah for a slave's present but unenforceable debt is instantly binding on the kafil.

In previous chapters, the rulings for kafalah involving free persons were discussed. Now we turn to the case of the slave. The central question: if a person guarantees a debt that the slave owes but cannot be forced to pay until after emancipation, and no condition is made about timing, is the kafalah valid and when is the kafil liable? This passage resolves the issue by analogy to guaranteeing for an absent or bankrupt person, establishing that the kafalah is present (hal) and the kafil must pay immediately, with recourse against the slave after freedom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The student identifies the specific case: a kafil guarantees a debt that is due now (the slave incurred a liability) but cannot be collected from the slave until he is free. The debt is 'present' (hal) in terms of obligation but 'deferred' in terms of enforceability.
Examples from the text: slave's dower from shubhah intercourse, or liability from a loan with master's permission.
2
Determine the default ruling for the kafalah
Since no mention of timing (present or deferred) is made, the kafalah is considered to be present (hal). The basis: the debt itself is present on the slave due to existence of cause (sabab) and acceptance of liability (qabul al-dhimma).
The slave's hardship does not delay the debt's existence; it only delays enforcement.
3
Analyze the impediment to pursuing the slave
The slave is not pursued now because he is in hardship (his property belongs to the master, who has not consented to the debt). The kafil, however, is not in hardship, so he can be demanded immediately.
This parallels the case of guaranteeing for an absent (gha'ib) or bankrupt (muflis) person.
4
Distinguish from deferred debt
The passage contrasts this with a deferred debt (dayn mu'ajjal), where the delay is contractual. Here the debt is not deferred by agreement; it is inherently due but unenforceable due to the slave's status.
Hence, the kafalah is not ta'jil (deferment) but hulul (presentness) for the kafil.
5
Apply the recourse rule
If the kafil pays, he can recover from the slave after emancipation, just as the original creditor could recover only after emancipation. The kafil stands in the creditor's place (qiyam maqamahu).
This is consistent with the general principle that the kafil is subrogated to the creditor's rights.

بابُ كفالة العَبُدوعَنهُ
كتاب الكفاله
تشريح سابقہ ابواب میں آزاد کے کفیل ہونے اور آزاد کی طرف سے کفیل ہونے کے احکام ذکر کئے گئے تھے اب اس آخری باب میں غلام کے کفیل ہونے اور غلام کی طرف سے کفیل ہونے کے احکام مذکور ہیں۔ اور بنو آدم میں چونکہ حریت اصل ہے اور آزاد ہی اشرف ہے اس لئے آزاد آدمی سے متعلق کفالہ کے احکام پہلے ذکر کئے گئے اور غلام سے متعلق کفالہ کے احکام بعد میں ذکر کئے گئے۔
ومن ضمن عن عبد مالا لايجب عليه حتى يُعتق ولم يسم حالا ولا غيره فهو حال لان المال حال عليه لوجود السبب وقبول الذمة الا انه لا يطالب به لعسرته اذ جميع مافی یده ملک المولى ولم يرض بتعلقه به في الحال والكفيل غير معسر فصار كما اذا كفل عن غائب او مفلس بخلاف الدين المؤجل لانه متاخر بمؤخر ثم اذا ادى رجع على العبد بعد العتق لان الطالب لا يرجع عليه الابعد العتق فكذا الكفيل لقيامه مقامه
ترجمہ اور اگر کوئی شخص کسی غلام کی طرف سے ایسے مال کا کفیل ہوا جو اس پر واجب الادا نہیں ہے یہاں تک کہ آزاد کیا جائے اور حال اور غیر حال کا ذکر نہیں کیا تو یہ کفالہ حالی ہے۔ اس لئے کہ غلام پر مال فی الحال واجب ہے کیونکہ سبب بھی موجود ہے اور قبول ذمہ بھی موجود ہے مگر یہ کہ غلام سے اس کی تنگدستی کی وجہ سے مطالبہ نہ کیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ جو کچھ غلام کے قبضہ میں ہے سب اس کے مولیٰ کی ملک ہے اور مولی فی الحال غلام کے ساتھ قرضہ متعلق ہونے پر راضی نہیں ہوا ہے۔ اور کفیل تنگدست بھی نہیں ہے۔ پس ایسا ہو گیا جیسے کسی غائب یا مفلس بتشدید [؟] کی طرف سے کفیل ہوا۔ برخلاف میعادی قرضہ کے۔ کیونکہ وہ مؤخر کرنے والے سب سے مؤخر ہے ۔ پھر جب کفیل نے (فی الحال ) ادا کیا تو وہ غلام سے آزاد ہونے کے بعد واپس لے گا۔ کیونکہ مکفول لہ بھی اس سے نہیں لے سکتا مگر آزاد ہونے کے بعد ۔ پس اسی طرح کفیل، کیونکہ مطالبہ میں کفیل ، مکفول لہ کے قائم مقام ہے۔
تشريح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص غلام کی طرف سے ایسے مال کا کفیل ہوا۔ جو مال غلام پر واجب تو فی الحال ہو گیا ہے لیکن اُس سے لیا جائے گا آزادی کے بعد اور کفالہ میں اس کا کوئی تذکرہ نہیں کیا گیا کہ کفیل سے فی الحال مطالبہ کیا جائے گا یا فی الحال مطالبہ نہیں کیا جائے گا تو کفیل فی الحال ماخوذ ہوگا۔ مثلاً غلام نے مولی کی اجازت کے بغیر نکاح کر کے یہ سمجھ کر کہ نکاح درست ہو گیا حالانکہ نکاح جائز نہیں ہوا۔ شبہ اس عورت سے وطی کر لی تو مال وطی یعنی عقر غلام پر فی الحال واجب ہو گیا لیکن فی الحال ماخوذ نہ ہوگا بلکہ آزاد ہونے کے بعد ماخوذ ہوگا۔ اور جیسے غلام نے کسی شخص کا مال تلف کرنے کا اقرار کیا حالانکہ مولیٰ نے اس کی تکذیب کی ہے یا کسی آدمی نے غلام کو قرض دیا یا اس کے ہاتھ مال فروخت کیا حالانکہ یہ غلام مجور ہے۔ تو ان صورتوں میں مال، فی الحال غلام کے ذمہ واجب ہو گیا لیکن ماخوذ آزاد ہونے کے بعد ہو سکتا ہے۔ پس غلام کی طرف سے ایسے مال کی کسی شخص نے کفالت کر لی اور اسکی کوئی تفصیل نہیں کی گئی کہ كفيل سے فی الحال
Chapter on the kafalah of the slave and on his behalf. Explanation: In previous chapters, the rulings of a freeperson being a kafil and being a kafil on behalf of a freeperson were mentioned. Now in this last chapter, the rulings of a slave being a kafil and being a kafil on behalf of a slave are mentioned. And among the children of Adam, since freedom is the origin and the free are nobler, therefore the rulings of kafalah related to a free person were mentioned first, and the rulings of kafalah related to a slave were mentioned later. And whoever guarantees for a slave for a wealth that is not obligatory upon him until he is freed, and he does not mention present or other, then it is present, because the wealth is present upon him due to the existence of the cause and the acceptance of the dhimma, except that he will not be demanded for it due to his hardship, since all that is in his hand is the property of the master, and the master has not consented to its attachment in the present, and the kafil is not in hardship. So it becomes as if he guaranteed for an absent or bankrupt, contrary to a deferred debt, because it is deferred by a deferrer. Then when he pays, he can recover from the slave after emancipation, because the claimant cannot recover from him except after emancipation, and likewise the kafil, because he stands in his place. Translation: And if a person becomes a kafil for a slave for such wealth that is not due upon him until he is freed, and he does not mention present or non-present, then this kafalah is present. Because the wealth is obligatory upon the slave in the present, since the cause exists and the acceptance of the dhimma exists, except that the slave will not be demanded due to his hardship. Because whatever is in the slave's possession is all the property of his master, and the master has not consented to the debt being attached in the present. And the kafil is not in hardship. So it becomes like guaranteeing for an absent or bankrupt. Contrary to a deferred debt, because it is deferred by a deferrer. Then when the kafil pays (in the present), he will take it back from the slave after emancipation. Because the makful lahu (creditor) also cannot take from him except after emancipation. Similarly, the kafil, because in demand, the kafil stands in the place of the makful lahu. Explanation: The scenario of the issue is that a person becomes a kafil for a slave for such wealth that has become obligatory upon the slave in the present, but it will be taken from him after emancipation, and no mention was made in the kafalah whether the kafil will be demanded in the present or not. Then the kafil will be taken in the present. For example, a slave married without the master's permission, thinking the marriage was valid, but the marriage was not valid; then he had intercourse with that woman in shubhah, so the wealth of intercourse i.e., mahr (dower) became obligatory upon the slave in the present, but he will not be taken in the present; rather he will be taken after emancipation. And likewise, if the slave confessed to destroying someone's wealth while the master denied it, or if someone gave a loan to the slave or sold wealth to him while the slave is a ma'dhun (permitted to trade). In these cases, the wealth became obligatory upon the slave in the present, but he can only be taken after emancipation. So if a person guarantees for such wealth on behalf of the slave, and no detail was given whether the kafil will be taken in the present... (text cuts off).
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on kafalah involving slaves. The author first explains the order of presentation: because freedom is the original state, rulings for free persons were discussed first, now for slaves. The core ruling: if a person becomes a kafil (guarantor) for a slave for a debt that is not immediately enforceable until after the slave is freed, and no condition of being instant or deferred is stated, the kafalah is considered instant (hal). The reason is that the debt is essentially due now (the cause and acceptance of dhimma exist), but the slave is not pursued due to his inability (his property belongs to the master who hasn't consented). Since the kafil is not insolvent, he can be demanded immediately, analogous to guaranteeing for an absent or bankrupt person. This differs from a deferred debt. If the kafil pays, he can recover from the slave after emancipation because the original creditor could only recover after emancipation. The passage then provides examples: a slave who marries without permission and commits zina bi shubhah owes the 'aqr (dower), but it is only collectible after freedom; similarly, a slave's confession or loans taken with master's permission. In all these, the debt is due now but payment is deferred until freedom, and the kafalah is considered instant.

Key Terms
غلام (ghulam): A male slave; in legal terms, a human being owned by another with limited capacity.
کفیل (kafil): The guarantor who assumes responsibility for a debt or presence of another.
مکفول لہ (makful lahu): The creditor or beneficiary in a kafalah contract.
عقر (aqr): The dower paid in case of intercourse under shubhah (mistaken belief of validity).
حال (hal): Present, due immediately; opposite of مؤجل (deferred).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person becomes a kafil for a slave's debt that is owed now but can only be collected after the slave is freed. No condition about timing is mentioned. What is the ruling on the kafalah?
A) Invalid because the slave cannot be pursued.
B) Deferred on the kafil until the slave is freed.
C) Present on the kafil; he can be demanded immediately.
D) Considered a gift, not a contractual guarantee.

Key Takeaway
When a kafalah is made for a slave's debt that is due but not immediately collectible, and no timing is specified, the kafil is immediately liable and can later recover from the slave after emancipation.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
6 min
Guarantor's Liability for a Slave's Immediately Due Debt
Ruling on guarantor's liability for a slave's immediately due debt, with qiyās to ghāʾib and muflis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the guarantor's liability when guaranteeing a slave's immediately due debt, and apply the analogy with suretyship for an absent or insolvent person.

Core Idea
A guarantor for a slave is immediately liable, even though the slave's debt is collected only after emancipation.

In this passage, al-Marghinani addresses a nuanced case of suretyship involving a slave. The key question: when a slave has a debt that is due immediately (ḥāl) but its collection is postponed due to the slave's hardship (ʿusr) because all his property belongs to the master, what is the position of the guarantor? The author argues that the guarantor, being free and solvent, does not share this impediment, and therefore must pay immediately. This ruling is derived through qiyās (analogy) with suretyship for an absent (ghāʾib) or insolvent (muflis) person.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
A guarantor undertakes to pay a debt that is immediately due on a slave, but collection from the slave is delayed until after emancipation.
The debt is not deferred by agreement; it is due now, but collection is temporarily impossible.
2
Determine the nature of the debt
Is the debt ḥāl or muʾajjal? The text clarifies it is ḥāl because the cause exists and the slave's dhimma accepts it.
The cause could be like marriage without permission leading to liability for mahr.
3
Analyze the impediment (māniʿ)
The impediment preventing collection from the slave is his ʿusratuhu: all property belongs to the master, and the master did not consent to the debt's attachment. This impediment does not apply to the guarantor.
The guarantor is not in hardship (ghayr muʿsir).
4
Apply qiyās
Compare with suretyship for ghāʾib or muflis: in both, the principal cannot be reached immediately, but the guarantor is liable. The effective cause (ʿillah) is that the debt is established on the principal's dhimma and is due immediately, but collection is hindered by a factor external to the guarantor.
The ʿillah is extracted by tanqīḥ al-manāṭ.
5
Distinguish from deferred debt
A deferred debt is postponed by agreement (taʾjīl). Here, postponement is due to an external hindrance, not agreement, so the guarantor's liability remains immediate.
The text explicitly uses 'bikhilāf al-dayn al-muʾajjal'.

كتاب الكفاله
تشریح سابقہ ابواب میں آزاد کے کفیل ہونے اور آزاد کی طرف سے کفیل ہونے کے احکام ذکر کئے گئے تھے اب اس آخری باب میں غلام کے کفیل ہونے اور غلام کی طرف سے کفیل ہونے کے احکام مذکور ہیں۔ اور بنو آدم میں چونکہ حریت اصل ہے اور آزاد ہی اشرف ہے اس لئے آزاد آدمی سے متعلق کفالہ کے احکام پہلے ذکر کئے گئے اور غلام سے متعلق کفالہ کے احکام بعد میں ذکر کئے گئے۔ ایک شخص غلام کی طرف سے مال کا کفیل ہوا تو غلام سے آزادی کے بعد وصول کیا جائے گا

ترجمہ اور اگر کوئی شخص کسی غلام کی طرف سے ایسے مال کا کفیل ہوا جو اس پر واجب الادا نہیں ہے یہاں تک کہ آزاد کیا جائے اور حال اور غیر حال کا ذکر نہیں کیا تو یہ کفالہ حالی ہے۔ اس لئے کہ غلام پر مال فی الحال واجب ہے کیونکہ سبب بھی موجود ہے اور قبول ذمہ بھی موجود ہے مگر یہ کہ غلام سے اس کی تنگدستی کی وجہ سے مطالبہ نہ کیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ جو کچھ غلام کے قبضہ میں ہے سب اس کے مولیٰ کی ملک ہے اور مولی فی الحال غلام کے ساتھ قرضہ متعلق ہونے پر راضی نہیں ہوا ہے۔ اور کفیل تنگدست بھی نہیں ہے۔ پس ایسا ہو گیا جیسے کسی غائب یا مفلس بتشدید السلام) کی طرف سے کفیل ہوا۔ برخلاف میعادی قرضہ کے۔ کیونکہ وہ مؤخر کرنے والے سب سے مؤخر ہے ۔ پھر جب کفیل نے (فی الحال ) ادا کیا تو وہ غلام سے آزاد ہونے کے بعد واپس لے گا۔ کیونکہ مکفول لہ بھی اس سے نہیں لے سکتا مگر آزاد ہونے کے بعد ۔ پس اسی طرح کفیل، کیونکہ مطالبہ میں کفیل ، مکفول لہ کے قائم مقام ہے۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص غلام کی طرف سے ایسے مال کا کفیل ہوا۔ جو مال غلام پر واجب تو فی الحال ہو گیا ہے لیکن اُس سے لیا جائے گا آزادی کے بعد اور کفالہ میں اس کا کوئی تذکرہ نہیں کیا گیا کہ کفیل سے فی الحال مطالبہ کیا جائے گا یا فی الحال مطالبہ نہیں کیا جائے گا تو کفیل فی الحال ماخوذ ہوگا۔ مثلاً غلام نے مولی کی اجازت کے بغیر نکاح کر کے یہ سمجھ کر کہ نکاح درست ہو گیا حالانکہ نکاح جائز نہیں ہوا۔ شبہ اس عورت سے وطی کر لی تو مال وطی یعنی عقر غلام پر فی الحال واجب ہو گیا لیکن فی الحال ماخوذ نہ ہوگا بلکہ آزاد ہونے کے بعد ماخوذ ہوگا۔ اور جیسے غلام نے کسی شخص کا مال تلف کرنے کا اقرار کیا حالانکہ مولیٰ نے اس کی تکذیب کی ہے یا کسی آدمی نے غلام کو قرض دیا یا اس کے ہاتھ مال فروخت کیا حالانکہ یہ غلام مجور ہے۔ تو ان صورتوں میں مال، فی الحال غلام کے ذمہ واجب ہو گیا لیکن ماخوذ آزاد ہونے کے بعد ہو سکتا ہے۔ پس غلام کی طرف سے ایسے مال کی کسی شخص نے کفالت کر لی اور اسکی کوئی تفصیل نہیں کی گئی کہ کفیل سے فی الحال
مطالبہ ہو گا یا فی الحال مطالبہ نہ ہو گا تو یہ مال کفیل پر فی الحال واجب الادا ہے یعنی مکفول کہ کفیل سے فی الحال مطالبہ کرنے کا مجاز ہے۔ اگر چه مکفول عنہ یعنی غلام سے آزادی کے بعد مطالبہ کا مجاز ہوگا۔ مذکورہ صورتوں میں کفالہ تو اس لئے درست ہے کہ کفیل اس مال کا کفیل ہوا ہے جو اصیل یعنی غلام پر مضمون ہے اور کفیل اس مال کو ادا کرنے پر قادر بھی ہے۔ اور اموال مضمونہ کا کفالہ چونکہ جائز ہے بشرطیکہ کفیل اس کو ادا کرنے پر قادر ہو اس لئے اس مال کا کفالہ جائز ہے۔ اور کفیل سے فی الحال مطالبہ کرنا اس لئے جائز ہے کہ مکفول عنہ یعنی غلام پر مال کا وجوب فی الحال ہے۔ اور غلام پر فی الحال، مال اس لئے واجب ہے کہ وجوب مال کا سبب یعنی بغیر مولی کی اجازت کے نکاح کر کے ولی کرنا وغیرہ پایا گیا ہے۔ اور غلام کا ذمہ بھی قبولیت مال کی صلاحیت رکھتا ہے۔ یعنی غلام کا ذمہ اس قابل ہے کہ اس پر مال ثابت کیا جا سکے۔ اور کوئی میعاد بھی موجود نہیں ہے۔ پس جب وجوب مال کا سبب بھی موجود ہے اور قبولیت ذمہ بھی موجود ہے اور کوئی اجل اور میعاد ذکر نہیں کی گئی تو غلام پر یہ مال فی الحال واجب ہوگا لیکن ایک مانع یعنی غلام کی تنگدستی کی وجہ سے اُس سے مطالبہ نہیں کیا جائے گا ۔ اور غلام اس لئے تنگدست ہے کہ جو کچھ غلام کے قبضہ میں ہے وہ سب مولیٰ کی ملک ہے اور مولیٰ اس بات پر بھی رضامند نہیں ہوا کہ اس کی ملک یعنی غلام کے ساتھ فی الحال کسی کا قرضہ متعلق ہو۔ پس جب غلام تنگدست ہے اور مولیٰ اس کے ساتھ قرضہ وغیر متعلق ہونے پر راضی نہیں ہوا تو مولی کے حق کی وجہ سے غلام سے فی الحال مطالبہ مؤخر کیا جائیگا۔ اور کفیل چونکہ تنگدست نہیں ہے اس لئے اس کے حق میں یہ مانع بھی موجود نہ ہوگا۔ اور جب کفیل کے حق میں مانع موجود نہیں ہے تو اس سے فی الحال مطالبہ کرنا بھی جائز ہوگا۔ اور یہ مسئلہ ایسا ہو گیا جیسے کسی غائب کی طرف سے کوئی شخص کفیل ہو گیا تو کفیل فی الحال ماخوذ ہوگا اگر چہ مکفول عنہ سے اس کی غیبت کی وجہ سے فی الحال وصول کرنا متعذر ہے اور یہ مفلکس کی طرف سے کفیل ہونے کی مانند ہو گیا۔ ملکس ( تشدید السلام ) اس کو کہتے ہیں جس سے مفلس ہونے کا قاضی نے اعلان کر دیا ہو۔ ایسے شخص سے کسی حقدار کو فی الحال مطالبہ کا اختیار نہیں ہوتا۔ پس اگر اس مفلس کی طرف سے کوئی شخص کفیل ہو گیا تو کفیل سے فی الحال مطالبہ ہوگا اگر چہ مکفول عنہ یعنی منکس سے فی الحال مطالبہ نہیں ہوگا بلکہ افلاس ختم ہونے کے بعد مطالبہ ہوگا۔ اسی طرح غلام سے اس کی تنگدستی اور حق مولی کی وجہ سے فی الحال مطالبہ نہیں ہوگا۔ لیکن کفیل سے فی الحال مطالبہ ہوگا۔
بخلاف الدين المؤجل سے ایک سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ جب غلام سے اُس کی آزادی کے بعد مال کا مطالبہ کیا جائے گا تو اس مال کو میعادی قرضہ" کیوں نہ قرار دیا جائے۔ یعنی یہ کہا جائے کہ یہ قرضہ غلام پر فی الحال واجب الادا نہیں ہے بلکہ مؤجل اور میعادی ہے۔ اور جب غلام یعنی مکفول عنہ پر قرضہ میعادی ہے۔ تو کفیل سے بھی میعاد پوری ہونے کے بعد مطالبہ کیا جائے گا ؟
جواب اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ دین مؤجل میں دین مکفول عنہ سے ایسے امر کی وجہ سے مؤخر ہے جو تاخیر کو واجب کرتا ہے اور وہ امر " تاجیل" ہے۔ اور کفیل نے اس کا التزام کیا ہے جو ملکول عنہ پر واجب ہے پس جب مکفول عنہ پر دین مؤجل ہے تو کفالہ بھی دین مؤجل کا ہوگا اور جب کفالہ دین موجل" کا ہوا تو کفیل سے فی الحال مطالبہ نہ ہو گا۔ بلکہ اجل اور میعاد کے بعد مطالبہ کا حق ہوگا۔ اور رہا متن کا مسئلہ تو اس میں غلام پر فی الحال ادا ئیگی واجب تھی لیکن ایک عذر یعنی اس کی تنگدستی اور مولیٰ کے حق کی وجہ سے فی الحال مطالبہ نہیں کیا گیا اور کفیل کے حق میں یہ عذر چونکہ موجود نہیں ہے اس لئے اُس سے فی الحال مطالبہ کرنے میں کوئی مضائقہ نہیں ہے۔
Chapter of Suretyship by and for a Slave. Translation: This chapter is about a slave being a guarantor and about giving guarantee on his behalf. Book of Suretyship. Explanation: In previous chapters, the rulings of suretyship for a free person and on behalf of a free person were mentioned. Now in this last chapter, the rulings of suretyship for a slave and on behalf of a slave are mentioned. Among the children of Adam, freedom is the original state and the free person is the nobler one, hence the rulings related to suretyship for a free person were mentioned first, and those related to a slave were mentioned later. If a person gives surety for a slave for a debt, it will be collected from the slave only after he is freed. And whoever gives surety for a slave for wealth that is not obligatory upon him until he is freed, and he does not specify it as immediate (hal) or otherwise, then it is immediate, because the wealth is immediately due on him due to the existence of the cause and the capacity of his dhimma (legal personality). However, he will not be demanded to pay because of his hardship, since all that is in his hand belongs to his master, and the master did not consent to it being attached to him (the slave) immediately. And the guarantor is not in hardship. So it becomes as if he gave surety for an absent person or an insolvent person. Contrary to a deferred debt, because that is postponed by a postponement. Then, if he (the guarantor) pays, he may recover from the slave after his emancipation, because the claimant does not have recourse against him except after emancipation, and likewise the guarantor, since he stands in his place. Translation and commentary: And if a person gives surety for a slave for such wealth that is not due upon him until he is freed, and he does not mention whether it is immediate or not, then this suretyship is immediate. This is because the wealth is immediately due on the slave, as the cause exists and the capacity of the dhimma exists. However, the slave will not be demanded to pay due to his hardship, because whatever is in the slave's possession belongs to his master, and the master has not consented to a debt being attached to the slave immediately. And the guarantor is not in hardship. So it becomes like when someone gives surety for an absent person or an insolvent person. Contrary to a deferred debt, because it is postponed due to a postponer. Then, when the guarantor pays (immediately), he will recover from the slave after emancipation, because the person in whose favor the guarantee is given cannot recover from him except after emancipation. Similarly, the guarantor, because in demanding payment, the guarantor stands in the place of the person in whose favor the guarantee is given. Explanation: The scenario of the issue is that a person gives surety for a slave for such wealth that has become immediately due on the slave but will be collected from him after emancipation, and in the suretyship it was not mentioned whether the guarantor will be demanded immediately or not, then the guarantor will be held immediately liable. For example, if a slave marries without his master's permission, thinking the marriage was valid, whereas it was not, and then he has intercourse with that woman under a mistaken assumption, then the payment for intercourse (uqr) becomes immediately due on the slave, but he will not be held liable immediately; rather, after emancipation. Similarly, if a slave acknowledges destroying someone's property while the master denies it, or if someone lends money to the slave or sells goods to him while the slave is authorized to trade, then in these cases the debt becomes immediately due on the slave, but can only be collected after emancipation. So if someone gives surety for the slave for such wealth, and no detail is given whether the guarantor will be demanded immediately or not, then this debt is immediately due on the guarantor, meaning the person in whose favor the guarantee is given has the right to demand immediate payment from the guarantor. Although he has the right to demand from the principal (the slave) only after emancipation. In these cases, the suretyship is valid because the guarantor has given surety for wealth that is established on the principal (the slave), and the guarantor is capable of paying it. Since suretyship for established debts is permissible provided the guarantor is capable of paying it, this suretyship is permissible. And demanding immediate payment from the guarantor is permissible because the obligation of the wealth on the principal (the slave) is immediate. The wealth is immediately due on the slave because the cause of obligation (such as marrying without permission and intercourse) is present, and the slave's dhimma has the capacity to accept obligation. Also, no term is specified. So when the cause of obligation exists, capacity of dhimma exists, and no term is mentioned, the wealth becomes immediately due on the slave, but due to an impediment (the slave's hardship) he will not be demanded to pay. The slave is in hardship because everything in his possession belongs to his master, and the master did not consent to a debt being attached to his property. So due to the master's right, demanding payment from the slave is postponed. Since the guarantor is not in hardship, this impediment does not exist for him, so it is permissible to demand immediate payment from him. This issue is like when a person gives surety for an absent person: the guarantor is held immediately liable even though it is impossible to collect from the absent principal due to his absence. And it is like giving surety for an insolvent person. "Mufallas" refers to one whom the judge has declared insolvent. The rightful claimant does not have the right to demand immediate payment from such a person. So if someone gives surety for this insolvent person, the guarantor will be demanded immediately, although the principal (the insolvent) will not be demanded until his insolvency ends. Similarly, the slave will not be demanded immediately due to his hardship and the master's right, but the guarantor will be demanded immediately. And "contrary to a deferred debt" is an answer to a question: The question is: Since the debt will be demanded from the slave after his emancipation, why is it not considered a deferred debt? That is, it could be said that this debt is not immediately due on the slave but is deferred. And if the debt on the principal (the slave) is deferred, then the guarantor should also be demanded only after the term expires? The answer is that in a deferred debt, the debt is postponed from the principal due to something that necessitates the delay, and that is the agreement to defer (ta'jil). And the guarantor undertakes what is obligatory on the principal. So when the principal has a deferred debt, the suretyship is also for a deferred debt, and the guarantor will not be demanded immediately, but only after the term. As for the issue in the text, the payment was immediately due on the slave, but due to an excuse (his hardship and the master's right) he was not demanded immediately, and since this excuse does not exist for the guarantor, there is no harm in demanding immediate payment from him.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on suretyship (kafalah) involving a slave. The author first states the general principle: if a person guarantees a debt that is immediately due on a slave but collection from the slave is delayed until after emancipation, the guarantee is considered immediate (hal) so that the guarantor can be demanded payment right away. The reasoning is that the debt is actually due on the slave's dhimma (legal capacity) because the cause exists (e.g., the slave committed an act that creates a liability) and the slave's dhimma can bear obligations. However, the slave cannot be forced to pay due to his 'usrah (hardship) since all his property belongs to his master and the master did not consent to the debt attaching to his property. The guarantor, being free and able to pay, does not share this impediment, so the person in whose favor the guarantee is given (makful lahu) may demand payment from the guarantor immediately. The author draws an analogy with suretyship for a missing person (gha'ib) or an insolvent person (muflis) who has been declared bankrupt by a judge. In those cases too, the principal cannot be reached immediately, but the guarantor is immediately liable. The passage distinguishes this from a genuinely deferred debt (dayn mu'ajjal), where the deferment is by agreement (ta'jil). In that case, the guarantor's liability is also deferred because the guarantor assumes what is upon the principal. The key difference: here the delay is due to an external impediment (slave's hardship, master's right), not an agreed term. The passage also provides illustrative examples: a slave marrying without permission and having intercourse under mistake (creating a liability for mahr or 'uqr), a slave acknowledging destruction of property (with master denying), or a slave receiving a loan or purchase while being authorized to trade (ma'dhun). In all these, the debt is immediately due on the dhimma but collection is postponed. The commentary emphasizes that the validity of the suretyship requires the guarantor to be able (qadir) to pay. The overall message is that the guarantor's liability is independent from the practical ability to collect from the principal; it follows the legal obligation (wujub) on the principal's dhimma, not the current collectibility.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafālah): A contract whereby a person (guarantor) undertakes to fulfill an obligation (usually a debt) on behalf of another (principal).
عبد (ʿabd): A male slave whose property and earnings belong to his master.
حال (ḥāl): Immediate; a debt or obligation that is due and payable at the present moment without any deferment.
دين مؤجل (dayn muʾajjal): A deferred debt; an obligation that is not payable until a specified future date agreed upon by the parties.
ذمة (dhimmah): Legal personality or capacity to bear rights and obligations.
عسرتہ (ʿusratuhū): His hardship or financial inability to pay; here specifically because the slave's property belongs to the master.
غائب (ghāʾib): An absent person; someone who is not present at the place of demand.
مفلس (muflis): An insolvent person who has been declared bankrupt by a judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when a guarantor guarantees a debt that is immediately due on a slave, but the slave cannot be forced to pay until after emancipation due to his hardship, the guarantor is:
A) Not liable until the slave is free.
B) Immediately liable.
C) Liable only if the guarantee was expressly stated as immediate.
D) Not liable because the guarantee is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The guarantor's liability is determined by the legal obligation on the principal's dhimma, not by the practical ability to collect; external impediments to collection do not delay the guarantor's duty to pay.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
6 min
Kafalah for Slave's Debt: Immediate Liability of Kafil
Establishes immediate liability of kafil for slave's debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that a kafil for a slave's debt can be sued immediately, and distinguish between a temporary impediment (mani') and a true deferment (ta'jil).

Core Idea
A kafil's liability is independent of the principal's personal obstacles; immediate demand is valid despite the slave's impediment.

When a slave incurs a debt and a kafil guarantees it, a key question arises: can the creditor demand payment from the kafil immediately, even though the slave himself cannot be sued due to poverty and the master's rights? The Hanafi answer is yes, and this lesson unpacks the reasoning behind that ruling. You will see how Hanafi jurists separate the underlying obligation from temporary impediments, and why this distinction is critical for the validity of kafalah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is kafalah for a debt owed by a slave. The ruling: the kafil is immediately liable and can be sued at once, even though the slave cannot be sued until after freedom.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
Analyze the rationale: why the debt is immediately due on the slave
The debt becomes wajib on the slave because its cause (e.g., a marriage contract without master's permission) exists, the slave's dhimma can bear obligations, and no term (ajal) was agreed. Thus the debt is due in principle.
Wujub al-ada' (immediate due-ness) is established even if actual collection is blocked.
3
Identify the impediment (mani') that blocks suing the slave
Two factors prevent immediate suit against the slave: (1) his poverty – all his property belongs to the master, and (2) the master's lack of consent to have a debt attached to his property. This is a temporary obstacle, not a deferment.
A mani' does not affect the underlying obligation; it only delays enforcement.
4
Understand why the mani' does not extend to the kafil
The kafil is free and solvent; the slave's poverty and the master's right are personal to the slave. The kafil has no such excuse, so immediate demand on him is permissible.
Analogous to kafalah for an absent person or a bankrupt: the kafil is immediately liable despite the principal's temporary inability.
5
Distinguish from deferred debt (dayn mu'ajjal)
If the debt were genuinely deferred by agreement, the kafil would also benefit from the term. But here, there is no agreement to postpone; only a factual impediment. Hence the kafil cannot claim deferment.
Ta'jil requires mutual consent; a mani' is a unilateral legal bar.

بخلاف الدين المؤجل سے ایک سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ جب غلام سے اُس کی آزادی کے بعد مال کا مطالبہ کیا جائے گا تو اس مال کو میعادی قرضہ" کیوں نہ قرار دیا جائے۔ یعنی یہ کہا جائے کہ یہ قرضہ غلام پر فی الحال واجب الادا نہیں ہے بلکہ مؤجل اور میعادی ہے۔ اور جب غلام یعنی مکفول عنہ پر قرضہ میعادی ہے۔ تو کفیل سے بھی میعاد پوری ہونے کے بعد مطالبہ کیا جائے گا ؟
جواب اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ دین مؤجل میں دین مکفول عنہ سے ایسے امر کی وجہ سے مؤخر ہے جو تاخیر کو واجب کرتا ہے اور وہ امر " تاجیل" ہے۔ اور کفیل نے اس کا التزام کیا ہے جو ملکول عنہ پر واجب ہے پس جب مکفول عنہ پر دین مؤجل ہے تو کفالہ بھی دین مؤجل کا ہوگا اور جب کفالہ دین موجل" کا ہوا تو کفیل سے فی الحال مطالبہ نہ ہو گا۔ بلکہ اجل اور میعاد کے بعد مطالبہ کا حق ہوگا۔ اور رہا متن کا مسئلہ تو اس میں غلام پر فی الحال ادا ئیگی واجب تھی لیکن ایک عذر یعنی اس کی تنگدستی اور مولیٰ کے حق کی وجہ سے فی الحال مطالبہ نہیں کیا گیا اور کفیل کے حق میں یہ عذر چونکہ موجود نہیں ہے اس لئے اُس سے فی الحال مطالبہ کرنے میں کوئی مضائقہ نہیں ہے۔
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ جب کھیل نے غلام کی طرف سے مال ادا کر دیا تو کفیل کو غلام کی آزادی کے بعد اس سے واپس لینے کا اختیار ہوگا۔ کیونکہ غلام سے خود مکفول لہ کو اس کی آزادی کے بعد مطالبہ کا حق ہے تو مکفول لہ کے قائم مقام یعنی کفیل کو بھی آزادی کے بعد مطالبہ کا حق ہوگا۔
Whether demand is made or not, this wealth is immediately due on the kafil, meaning the creditor (makful) is entitled to demand from the kafil immediately, even though demand from the principal (the slave) will only be possible after his freedom. In the mentioned scenarios, the kafalah is valid because the kafil became surety for the wealth that is owed by the principal (the slave), and the kafil is able to pay it. And since kafalah for guaranteed wealth is permissible provided the kafil is able to pay, this kafalah is permissible. Immediate demand from the kafil is permissible because the wealth is immediately due on the principal (the slave). The wealth is immediately due on the slave because the cause of obligation (marrying a wife without the master's permission and acting as a guardian, etc.) is present. And the slave's dhimma (capacity to bear obligations) is capable of accepting wealth, i.e., it is valid for wealth to be established against him. There is also no term (ajal) mentioned. Since the cause of obligation and the capacity of the dhimma exist, and no term is stated, the wealth is immediately due on the slave. However, due to a preventing factor (maani')—the slave's poverty—he cannot be sued. The slave is poor because whatever he possesses belongs to his master, and the master does not consent to any debt being immediately attached to his property (the slave). Because the slave is poor and the master does not consent to debt being attached, demand from the slave is postponed due to the master's right. But the kafil is not poor, so this preventing factor does not apply to him. Therefore, immediate demand from the kafil is permissible. This case is like when someone becomes a kafil for an absent person: the kafil is immediately proceeded against even though the principal cannot be reached due to his absence. It is also like becoming a kafil for a bankrupt person. 'Mafalis' (with shadda on lam) refers to one whom the judge has declared bankrupt. A claimant cannot immediately demand from such a person. But if someone becomes a kafil for this bankrupt, immediate demand is made from the kafil, even though the principal (the bankrupt) is not demanded until the bankruptcy ends. Similarly, the slave is not immediately demanded due to his poverty and the master's right, but the kafil is immediately demanded.
This is the answer to the question about deferred debt (dayn mu'ajjal). The question: Since the slave will only be demanded after his freedom, why not consider this debt as deferred? That is, say this debt is not immediately due on the slave but is deferred with a term. And when the debt on the slave (the principal) is deferred, then demand should also be from the kafil only after the term expires? The answer: In a deferred debt, the debt on the principal is postponed due to a factor that necessitates delay, and that factor is 'ta'jil' (making it deferred). The kafil has undertaken that which is due on the principal, so when the principal has a deferred debt, the kafalah is also for a deferred debt. Thus, the kafil cannot be demanded immediately; the right to demand arises only after the term. As for the issue in the text (matn), the debt was immediately due on the slave, but due to an excuse (his poverty and the master's right) immediate demand was not made. Since this excuse does not exist for the kafil, there is no harm in demanding from him immediately.
The author of al-Hidayah says: When the kafil pays the debt on behalf of the slave, he has the right to reclaim it from the slave after the slave's freedom. Because the creditor (makful lahu) has the right to demand from the slave after freedom, so the kafil, who stands in his place, also has the right to demand after freedom.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah is a commentary expanding on a ruling from al-Hidayah regarding kafalah for a debt owed by a slave. The passage type is a ruling with proof, using rational argument (dalil aqli) to distinguish between a true deferment (ta'jil) and a temporary obstacle. The author first establishes that the debt on the slave is immediately due in principle because its cause exists and the slave's dhimma can bear it, but collection is postponed due to the master's right and the slave's poverty. However, the kafil, being free and solvent, cannot hide behind this obstacle. The author then anticipates an objection: Why not classify this as a deferred debt (dayn mu'ajjal) and thus require waiting until the slave's freedom? He answers clearly: deferment is a mutually agreed term that the kafil also implicitly accepts; here there is no agreed term—only a factual impediment. The kafil's obligation remains immediately enforceable. The passage concludes with a practical consequence: if the kafil pays, he can recover from the slave after the latter's freedom, just as the original creditor could.

Key Terms
مكفول عنہ (makful 'anhu): The principal debtor (here, the slave) for whom the kafil becomes surety.
مانع (mani'): A legal impediment that temporarily prevents execution of the right, without affecting the underlying obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can the kafil for a slave's debt be sued immediately even though the slave himself cannot be sued?
A) Because the kafil is richer than the slave.
B) Because the slave's debt is deferred, so the kafil is liable immediately.
C) Because the impediment (slave's poverty and master's right) is personal to the slave and does not extend to the kafil.
D) Because the kafil can afford to pay the debt from his own wealth.

Key Takeaway
The kafil's obligation is immediately enforceable even when the principal faces a personal impediment, as long as the debt itself is due.

Discharge of the Kafil on Death of the Slave Principal
Ruling with proof by analogy (qiyas jaliy)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the death of the principal (mukful binnafs) discharges the kafil in kafalah bi nafsihi, and explain the qiyas jaliy used to derive this ruling.

Core Idea
In kafalah bi nafsihi, the kafil's liability is derivative — when the principal is discharged by death, the kafil is automatically discharged.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a specific scenario: a person claims a debt from a slave, and a third party becomes a kafil bi nafsihi (guarantor of the slave's presence). When the slave dies, the question arises: does the kafil remain liable? The text rules that the kafil is discharged, drawing a clear analogy to the case of a free principal. Understanding this ruling illuminates the subsidiary nature of kafalah and the logical endpoint of the guarantor's obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario and parties
Read the scenario: a creditor claims a debt from a slave (asl, the principal debtor). A third person becomes a kafil bi nafsihi for the creditor, meaning he guarantees to produce the slave in court or for detention. Then the slave dies.
2
Determine the ruling from the text
The text states: 'the kafil is discharged'. The kafil is free from any further obligation regarding the slave's appearance. No debt payment is owed by the kafil; the guarantee of presence ends.
3
Trace the reasoning: the clear analogy (qiyas jaliy)
The text compares to the case where the principal (mukful binnafs) is a free person. If a free principal dies, the kafil is discharged. Here, the principal is a slave, but death has the same effect: the obligation of the principal is extinguished, and the kafil's subsidiary liability also ends.
The asl (original case) is the free principal dying; the far' (new case) is the slave principal dying.
4
Extract the illa (effective cause)
The illa is the extinction of the principal's obligation due to death. Death ends the need to produce the person because the underlying claim (debt) is no longer actionable against the deceased. The kafil's role is only to guarantee presence, not to pay the debt.
The illa is jaliyyah (clear) and is extracted by tanqih al-manat: death is the common, relevant factor.
5
Confirm the ruling and its scope
The derived ruling is that the kafil is discharged regardless of the principal's free or slave status. The kafalah bi nafsihi is not affected by the principal's legal capacity; death terminates the guarantee.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کسی نے غلام پر مال کا دعوی کیا اور بدعی کے لئے ایک شخص کفیل بنفسہ ہو گیا، غلام فوت ہو گیا تو کفیل بھی بری ہو جائے گا
ومن ادعى على عبد مالا وكفل له رجل بنفسه فمات العبد برئ الكفيل البراءة الاصيل كما اذا كان المكفول بنفسه حرا
ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے غلام پر مال کا دعویٰ کیا اور مدعی کے لئے ایک شخص کفیل بنفسہ ہو گیا پھر یہ غلام مرگیا تو کفیل بری ہو گیا کیونکہ اصیل بری ہو گیا ہے جیسے اگر مکفول بالنفس کوئی آزاد ہو۔
تشریح مسلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص نے غلام پر مال کا دعوی کیا اور دوسرا شخص مدعی کے لئے غلام کی طرف سے کفیل بنفسہ ہو گیا یعنی غلام کو حاضر کرنے کی ذمہ داری قبول کر لی پھر غلام یعنی مکفول بنفسہ مر گیا تو کفیل بری ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ موت کی وجہ سے اصیل (مکفول بنفسہ ) بری ہو گیا ہے اور اصیل کی برکت سے کفیل بری ہو جاتا ہے۔ جیسا کہ اگر مکفول بنفہ آزاد ہوتا اور بری ہو جاتا تو اس کے ہری ہونے سے کفیل بری ہو جاتا ہے۔ پس اسی طرح یہاں بھی مکفول بنفہ غلام کے بری ہونے سے کفیل بری ہو جائے گا۔ حاصل یہ کہ مکفول بنفسہ ، آزاد ہو یا غلام ہو کفالہ بالنفس متفاوت نہیں ہوتا۔ یعنی دونوں صورتوں میں مکفول بنفسہ کے مرنے سے چونکہ خود اصیل (مکفول بنفسہ ) بری ہو جاتا ہے۔ اس لئے کفیل بھی بری ہو جائے گا۔
Someone claimed a debt against a slave, and another person became a kafil bi nafsihi for the claimant. The slave died, then the kafil is also discharged, because the principal (asl) is discharged, just as if the principal in kafalah bi nafsihi were a free person.
Translation: And if someone claimed a debt against a slave, and a man became a kafil bi nafsihi for the claimant, then the slave died, the kafil is discharged because the principal is discharged, just as if the mukful binnafs had been free.
Explanation: The issue is that a person claimed a debt against a slave, and another person became a kafil bi nafsihi for the claimant on behalf of the slave, i.e., he accepted the responsibility to produce the slave. Then the slave (the principal in kafalah bi nafsihi) died, so the kafil is discharged. Because the principal (mukful binnafs) is discharged due to death, and the kafil is discharged by the discharge of the principal. Just as if the principal had been free and had become discharged, then the kafil would be discharged by his discharge. Similarly here, by the discharge of the slave (who is the principal), the kafil is discharged. The conclusion is that the principal in kafalah bi nafsihi, whether free or slave, does not differ; in both cases, when the principal dies, he is discharged, and so the kafil is also discharged.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof by analogy (qiyas jaliy). It presents a case from al-Hidayah: when a person claims a debt from a slave, and a third party becomes a kafil bi nafsihi (surety for the person's presence) for the claimant, and then the slave dies, the kafil is discharged. The reason is that the principal's obligation (to appear in court or settle the debt) is extinguished by death, and the kafil's liability is derivative—it follows the principal. The commentary emphasizes that this rule applies equally to free persons and slaves; the nature of kafalah bi nafsihi does not change based on the principal's status. The proof is by analogy to the clear case (qiyas jaliy) of a free principal: if the principal is freed (or dies), the kafil is discharged.

Key Terms
كفالة بالنفس (kafalah bi nafsihi): A surety where the kafil guarantees the physical presence of the person (to appear in court or for detention).
اصیل (asl): The principal debtor (the person whose obligation is guaranteed).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a kafil bi nafsihi guarantees the presence of a slave for a debt claim, and the slave dies, what happens to the kafil?
A) The kafil remains liable to pay the debt.
B) The kafil is discharged because the principal's obligation is extinguished.
C) The kafil must produce the slave's corpse in court.
D) The kafil is liable for the slave's value.

Key Takeaway
In kafalah bi nafsihi, death of the principal (whether free or slave) extinguishes the kafil's obligation to produce the person, by clear analogy to the free case.
